  <link rel="canonical" href="https://journals.plos.org/plosone/article?id=10.1371/journal.pone.0022728" />
  <meta name="description" content="Cucumber (Cucumis sativus L.), a widely cultivated crop, has originated from Eastern Himalayas and secondary domestication regions includes highly divergent climate conditions e.g. temperate and subtropical. We wanted to uncover adaptive genome differences between the cucumber cultivars and what sort of evolutionary molecular mechanisms regulate genetic adaptation of plants to different ecosystems and organism biodiversity. Here we present the draft genome sequence of the Cucumis sativus genome of the North-European Borszczagowski cultivar (line B10) and comparative genomics studies with the known genomes of: C. sativus (Chinese cultivar &ndash; Chinese Long (line 9930)), Arabidopsis thaliana, Populus trichocarpa and Oryza sativa. Cucumber genomes show extensive chromosomal rearrangements, distinct differences in quantity of the particular genes (e.g. involved in photosynthesis, respiration, sugar metabolism, chlorophyll degradation, regulation of gene expression, photooxidative stress tolerance, higher non-optimal temperatures tolerance and ammonium ion assimilation) as well as in distributions of abscisic acid-, dehydration- and ethylene-responsive cis-regulatory elements (CREs) in promoters of orthologous group of genes, which lead to the specific adaptation features. Abscisic acid treatment of non-acclimated Arabidopsis and C. sativus seedlings induced moderate freezing tolerance in Arabidopsis but not in C. sativus. This experiment together with analysis of abscisic acid-specific CRE distributions give a clue why C. sativus is much more susceptible to moderate freezing stresses than A. thaliana. Comparative analysis of all the five genomes showed that, each species and/or cultivars has a specific profile of CRE content in promoters of orthologous genes. Our results constitute the substantial and original resource for the basic and applied research on environmental adaptations of plants, which could facilitate creation of new crops with improved growth and yield in divergent conditions." />

  <meta name="citation_abstract" content="Cucumber (Cucumis sativus L.), a widely cultivated crop, has originated from Eastern Himalayas and secondary domestication regions includes highly divergent climate conditions e.g. temperate and subtropical. We wanted to uncover adaptive genome differences between the cucumber cultivars and what sort of evolutionary molecular mechanisms regulate genetic adaptation of plants to different ecosystems and organism biodiversity. Here we present the draft genome sequence of the Cucumis sativus genome of the North-European Borszczagowski cultivar (line B10) and comparative genomics studies with the known genomes of: C. sativus (Chinese cultivar &ndash; Chinese Long (line 9930)), Arabidopsis thaliana, Populus trichocarpa and Oryza sativa. Cucumber genomes show extensive chromosomal rearrangements, distinct differences in quantity of the particular genes (e.g. involved in photosynthesis, respiration, sugar metabolism, chlorophyll degradation, regulation of gene expression, photooxidative stress tolerance, higher non-optimal temperatures tolerance and ammonium ion assimilation) as well as in distributions of abscisic acid-, dehydration- and ethylene-responsive cis-regulatory elements (CREs) in promoters of orthologous group of genes, which lead to the specific adaptation features. Abscisic acid treatment of non-acclimated Arabidopsis and C. sativus seedlings induced moderate freezing tolerance in Arabidopsis but not in C. sativus. This experiment together with analysis of abscisic acid-specific CRE distributions give a clue why C. sativus is much more susceptible to moderate freezing stresses than A. thaliana. Comparative analysis of all the five genomes showed that, each species and/or cultivars has a specific profile of CRE content in promoters of orthologous genes. Our results constitute the substantial and original resource for the basic and applied research on environmental adaptations of plants, which could facilitate creation of new crops with improved growth and yield in divergent conditions.">

        <div class="article-text" id="artText">
          



<div class="abstract toc-section abstract-type-"><a id="abstract0" name="abstract0" data-toc="abstract0" class="link-target" title="Abstract"></a><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="abstract-content"><a id="article1.front1.article-meta1.abstract1.p1" name="article1.front1.article-meta1.abstract1.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>Cucumber (<em>Cucumis sativus</em> L.), a widely cultivated crop, has originated from Eastern Himalayas and secondary domestication regions includes highly divergent climate conditions e.g. temperate and subtropical. We wanted to uncover adaptive genome differences between the cucumber cultivars and what sort of evolutionary molecular mechanisms regulate genetic adaptation of plants to different ecosystems and organism biodiversity. Here we present the draft genome sequence of the <em>Cucumis sativus</em> genome of the North-European Borszczagowski cultivar (line B10) and comparative genomics studies with the known genomes of: <em>C. sativus</em> (Chinese cultivar – Chinese Long (line 9930)), <em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em>, <em>Populus trichocarpa</em> and <em>Oryza sativa</em>. Cucumber genomes show extensive chromosomal rearrangements, distinct differences in quantity of the particular genes (e.g. involved in photosynthesis, respiration, sugar metabolism, chlorophyll degradation, regulation of gene expression, photooxidative stress tolerance, higher non-optimal temperatures tolerance and ammonium ion assimilation) as well as in distributions of abscisic acid-, dehydration- and ethylene-responsive <em>cis</em>-regulatory elements (CREs) in promoters of orthologous group of genes, which lead to the specific adaptation features. Abscisic acid treatment of non-acclimated <em>Arabidopsis</em> and <em>C. sativus</em> seedlings induced moderate freezing tolerance in <em>Arabidopsis</em> but not in <em>C. sativus</em>. This experiment together with analysis of abscisic acid-specific CRE distributions give a clue why <em>C. sativus</em> is much more susceptible to moderate freezing stresses than <em>A. thaliana</em>. Comparative analysis of all the five genomes showed that, each species and/or cultivars has a specific profile of CRE content in promoters of orthologous genes. Our results constitute the substantial and original resource for the basic and applied research on environmental adaptations of plants, which could facilitate creation of new crops with improved growth and yield in divergent conditions.</p>
</div></div>


<div class="articleinfo"><p><strong>Citation: </strong>Wóycicki R, Witkowicz J, Gawroński P, Dąbrowska J, Lomsadze A, Pawełkowicz M, et al.  (2011) The Genome Sequence of the North-European Cucumber (<em>Cucumis sativus</em> L.) Unravels Evolutionary Adaptation Mechanisms in Plants. PLoS ONE 6(7):
           e22728.
        
        https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0022728</p><p><strong>Editor: </strong>Miguel A. Blazquez, Instituto de Biología Molecular y Celular de Plantas, Spain</p><p><strong>Received: </strong>November 18, 2010; <strong>Accepted: </strong>July 5, 2011; <strong>Published: </strong> July 28, 2011</p><p><strong>Copyright: </strong> © 2011 Wóycicki et al. This is an open-access article distributed under the terms of the Creative Commons Attribution License, which permits unrestricted use, distribution, and reproduction in any medium, provided the original author and source are credited.</p><p><strong>Funding: </strong>This work has been funded by grants from the Polish Ministry of Science and Higher Education: PZB-MNiSW-2/3/2006/36 (representing part of grant PZB-MNiSW-2/3/2006), NN302429734, NN302363333, Welcome 2008/1 - a project operated within the Foundation for Polish Science Welcome Program and co-financed by the European Regional Development Fund, and U.S. National Institutes of Health grant GM47853. The funders had no role in study design, data collection and analysis, decision to publish, or preparation of the manuscript.</p><p><strong>Competing interests: </strong> The authors have declared that no competing interests exist.</p></div>





<div id="section1" class="section toc-section"><a id="s1" name="s1" data-toc="s1" class="link-target" title="Introduction"></a><h2>Introduction</h2><a id="article1.body1.sec1.p1" name="article1.body1.sec1.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>The cultivated cucumber (<em>Cucumis sativus</em> L.), an important species for the global food market (<a href="http://faostat.fao.org">http://faostat.fao.org</a>), represents a model organism for investigations of organellar genetics <a href="#pone.0022728-Havey1" class="ref-tip">[1]</a>–<a href="#pone.0022728-Bartoszewski1" class="ref-tip">[3]</a>, flower sex determination <a href="#pone.0022728-Malepszy1" class="ref-tip">[4]</a>, mechanisms of somatic embryogenesis <a href="#pone.0022728-Zikowska1" class="ref-tip">[5]</a>–<a href="#pone.0022728-Malinowski1" class="ref-tip">[7]</a> epigenetics of various stresses in tissue culture <a href="#pone.0022728-adyyski1" class="ref-tip">[8]</a>, <a href="#pone.0022728-Filipecki1" class="ref-tip">[9]</a> and various aspects of chloroplast gene regulation (e.g. editing, tilling macroarray) <a href="#pone.0022728-Plder1" class="ref-tip">[10]</a>, <a href="#pone.0022728-GuzowskaNowowiejska1" class="ref-tip">[11]</a>. Genetic and cultivation research of that species is conducted by many international groups including the International Cucurbit Genomics Initiative (ICuGI) – (<a href="http://www.icugi.org/cgi-bin/ICuGI/misc/project.cgi">http://www.icugi.org/cgi-bin/ICuGI/misc/project.cgi</a>); and the USDA-ARS at the University of Wisconsin, Madison. Cucumber is cultivated in habitats ranging from the Northern regions of the temperate climate zone to the tropics. Spatial isolation and the need to adapt to diverse environmental conditions have induced a requirement for various adaptation mechanisms based upon polymorphisms within the 367 Mbp cucumber genome <a href="#pone.0022728-Arumuganathan1" class="ref-tip">[12]</a>. These factors have inspired us to undertake the <em>de novo</em> sequencing of genome of the Northern European cucumber. While this work was in progress the genomic sequence of the Chinese Long cultivar of cucumber which is known as line 9930 became available <a href="#pone.0022728-Huang1" class="ref-tip">[13]</a>. This has made original comparative studies between the two cultivars possible.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec1.p2" name="article1.body1.sec1.p2" class="link-target"></a><p>The evolution of organisms is driven by their need to reproduce as efficiently as possible in a given habituate. The optimization of that Darwinian fitness is known to include mechanisms of, for example, point mutations and chromosomal rearrangements leading to changes in gene quality and quantity as well as <em>cis</em>-regulatory element shuffling, which facilitate formation of new species and varieties. The speed of evolution is dependent on the stringency of abiotic and biotic stresses and on the organism ability to respond to the given stringency. Highly divergent environmental conditions of species domestication, which are different from its place of origin, speed up this process. It is known that, abiotic stresses caused by starvation, high or low temperatures, UV radiation or treatment with hydrogen peroxide cause chromosomal rearrangements in the form of translocations, deletions, inversions, amplifications and chromosome fusions, as well as aneuploidy. These chromosomal rearrangements are stabilized over the generations and through changes in gene content lead to acquisition of resistance, as it was shown in e.g. yeast and plants <a href="#pone.0022728-Coyle1" class="ref-tip">[14]</a>–<a href="#pone.0022728-Cullis1" class="ref-tip">[20]</a>. Changes in regulation of gene expression due to regulatory networks rewiring could constitute other and faster mechanism of adaptations. Plants and animals have homologous and analogous regulatory mechanisms of stress responses, which include the selective activation/deactivation of appropriate trans-regulatory factors (TRFs) that specifically interact with <em>cis</em>-regulatory elements (CREs), in order to induce or suppress gene expression. This leads to the establishment of a new optimal adaptation status <a href="#pone.0022728-Muhlenbock1" class="ref-tip">[21]</a>–<a href="#pone.0022728-Koussevitzky1" class="ref-tip">[24]</a>.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec1.p3" name="article1.body1.sec1.p3" class="link-target"></a><p>CREs are short DNA sequences generally composed of several nucleotides in the promoter region of a gene, for which TRFs have specific affinity. CREs and TRFs provide the molecular basis for transcriptional regulation both in plants and in animals. Induction or suppression of genes expression may depend upon the presence of single CRE or combinations of CREs as well as the orientation of CREs with respect to the gene <a href="#pone.0022728-YamaguchiShinozaki1" class="ref-tip">[25]</a>, <a href="#pone.0022728-Geisler1" class="ref-tip">[26]</a>. The best characterized CREs in plants include abscisic acid responsive element (ABRE (sequence – <span class="gene">ACGTGTC</span>)) which, as implied by its name, is linked to the response to abscisic acid (ABA), dehydration responsive element (DRE (also known as CRT), sequence - A/<span class="gene">GCCGAC</span>), an element induced by drought, salinity and frost, and ethylene responsive element (ERE (also known as GCC-box), sequence – <span class="gene">AGCCGCC</span>), an element linked to the response to ethylene. Abscisic acid is a hormone which plays an important role in the integrated response of plants to stresses caused by drought, cold, excessive light, pathogen attack and salinity <a href="#pone.0022728-McCourt1" class="ref-tip">[27]</a>, <a href="#pone.0022728-Yasuda1" class="ref-tip">[28]</a>. Ethylene plays an important role in regulation of many aspects of plant life including flowering, fruit ripening, root hypoxia responses, and other programmed cell death-dependent processes <a href="#pone.0022728-Muhlenbock1" class="ref-tip">[21]</a>, <a href="#pone.0022728-Muhlenbock2" class="ref-tip">[29]</a>.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec1.p4" name="article1.body1.sec1.p4" class="link-target"></a><p>Here we present the draft genome sequence of northern European cucumber cultivar Borszczagowski line B10 assembled by a hybrid method, structural and functional annotations and its comparisons with the highly divergent <a href="#pone.0022728-Staub1" class="ref-tip">[30]</a> Asian cucumber cultivar Chinese Long line 9930 <a href="#pone.0022728-Huang1" class="ref-tip">[13]</a> and the genomes of three other species (<em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em>, <em>Populus trichocarpa</em>, <em>Oryza sativa</em>). Shotgun sequencing was performed using the 454 Titanium technique at 12× genome coverage (8× genome coverage with single reads and 4× genome coverage with paired reads (3 Kbp insert size fragments). Additionally, the ends of inserts of a genomic BAC library were sequenced using the Sanger method. We analyzed SNP/INDEL polymorphism, genome rearrangements, compared functional groups of genes between the two cucumbers and also performed comparative analysis of the above CREs content for all the above five species.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec1.p5" name="article1.body1.sec1.p5" class="link-target"></a><p>Presented here comparative genomic studies of five plant genomes provide a strong support for the previous suggestions <a href="#pone.0022728-Hanikenne1" class="ref-tip">[31]</a>, <a href="#pone.0022728-Seliverstov1" class="ref-tip">[32]</a> that optimization of the Darwinian fitness of a given species or variety is determined by the evolution of the content of the genome (expressed as changes in number and quality of genes) and mutations in some CRE. However, our results suggests that species- or even cultivar-specific evolution of ABRE, DRE and ERE regulons, due to extensive CRE shuffling is faster than the evolution of the gene introns and exons. Such comparative genomic studies that also include CRE content analysis in groups of orthologous gene promoters supported by experiments will help in finding of new biotechnological solutions for a specific improvement of crops performance.</p>
</div>

<div id="section2" class="section toc-section"><a id="s2" name="s2" data-toc="s2" class="link-target" title="Results"></a><h2>Results</h2>
<div id="section1" class="section toc-section"><a id="s2a" name="s2a" class="link-target" title="Sequencing of the cucumber genome"></a>
<h3>Sequencing of the cucumber genome</h3>

<div id="section1" class="section toc-section"><a id="s2a1" name="s2a1" class="link-target" title="Bacterial artificial chromosome (BAC) library"></a><h4>Bacterial artificial chromosome (BAC) library.</h4><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec1.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec1.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>The number of clones of long genomic cucumber fragments derived from two libraries (<em>Mbo</em>I/<em>BamH</em>I and <em>Hind</em>III) was 33,792. The average length of the library inserts was 130 Kbp and the coverage of the genome with library BAC clones was 11.97×.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec1.p2" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec1.p2" class="link-target"></a><p>The basic characteristics of the BAC genomic library of line B10, such as average insert length and genome coverage, are comparable with the upper values of libraries previously described for plant species with similar genome sizes <a href="#pone.0022728-Kasprzak1" class="ref-tip">[33]</a>–<a href="#pone.0022728-Winters1" class="ref-tip">[36]</a>. Three other cucumber BAC libraries contained shorter inserts with an average length of 105 Kbp in line S94 <a href="#pone.0022728-Guan1" class="ref-tip">[37]</a>, 107 Kbp and 100.8 Kbp in the Suiseifushinari 2-go variety <a href="#pone.0022728-Nam1" class="ref-tip">[38]</a> and also had lower (2–2.5 fold) coverage of the genome and a smaller number of clones (10,000 to 15,000).</p>
</div>

<div id="section2" class="section toc-section"><a id="s2a2" name="s2a2" class="link-target" title="BAC end sequences (BESs)"></a><h4>BAC end sequences (BESs).</h4><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec2.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec2.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>Among the 33,792 inserts of the BAC library obtained after sequencing with the Sanger method, 66,776 sequences of clone ends were obtained (98.80% of the number of ends) (<a href="#pone.0022728.s018">Table S1</a>). Analysis of these sequences indicated the presence of 64,610 (96.76%) good quality fragments, without foreign DNA contamination. The percentage of chloroplast and mitochondrial genome sequences was 3.24%, and 0.46%, respectively. The remaining 62,217 (93.17%) ends of library clones were defined as sequences of nuclear origin (accession numbers: FI132140–FI136208, GS765762–GS766880, GS815969–GS874855). The average read length was 737 bp, and the total number of nucleotides in sequences of nuclear BESs was 45,853,929 bp, which corresponds to 12.49% of the genome. The average distance between sequences of BAC ends, which constitute unique genomic markers, was found to be 5500 bp. The participation of the obtained BAC end sequences and specifically of the paired ones was greater than 90% in each case and respectively 11% and 8% greater than the percentages described in previous publications <a href="#pone.0022728-Tomkins1" class="ref-tip">[39]</a>, <a href="#pone.0022728-Hong1" class="ref-tip">[40]</a>. The number of organelle sequences present in the BESs was found to be similar to the numbers of sequences described in previous reports <a href="#pone.0022728-Lacombe1" class="ref-tip">[41]</a>–<a href="#pone.0022728-Ammiraju1" class="ref-tip">[43]</a>. Two different libraries of the cucumber variety Suiseifushinari 2-go <a href="#pone.0022728-Nam1" class="ref-tip">[38]</a> contained 0.47% chloroplast sequences in <em>BamH</em>I clones and 0.26% chloroplast sequences in <em>EcoR</em>I clones. These libraries also had 0.40% mitochondrial sequences in <em>BamH</em>I clones and 0.20% mitochondrial sequences in <em>EcoR</em>I clones.</p>
</div>

<div id="section3" class="section toc-section"><a id="s2a3" name="s2a3" class="link-target" title="Sequence reads after pyrosequencing"></a><h4>Sequence reads after pyrosequencing.</h4><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec3.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec3.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>The total number of fragments after paired (PA) and unpaired (UN) library ends sequencing was over 15 million with a total length of almost 4.5 billion bp. This represents 12× coverage of the genome. Purified sequences had an average length of 290 bp. The longest sequences (374 bp) were from the library of unpaired fragments and the shortest sequences (171 bp) were from the library of paired ends. Lengths and statistics of reads after pyrosequencing are presented in <a href="#pone.0022728.s004">Figures S1A and S1B</a> and <a href="#pone.0022728.s019">Table S2</a>. The reads obtained after sequencing the library of paired fragments were divided into sequences which were treated as: (a) unpaired because of: the absence of a linker, the occurrence of only a part of it, or the presence of many linker sequences, as well as (b) paired reads which gave the lowest coverage (1.50×).</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec3.p2" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec3.p2" class="link-target"></a><p>The coverage generated by sequence reads was the lowest of those used so far in <em>de novo</em> sequencing of higher organisms using next generation sequencing technologies <a href="#pone.0022728-Diguistini1" class="ref-tip">[44]</a>–<a href="#pone.0022728-Reinhardt1" class="ref-tip">[46]</a>, but similar to 15× coverage recommended for as the minimal by 454 Life Sciences (Bradford, CT, USA) (<a href="http://454.com/downloads/DeNovoComplexGenomes_Flyer.pdf">http://454.com/downloads/DeNovoComplexGenomes_Flyer.pdf</a>). A similar or lower coverage has only been used in sequencing of transcriptomes <a href="#pone.0022728-Meyer1" class="ref-tip">[47]</a>, <a href="#pone.0022728-Vera1" class="ref-tip">[48]</a>, selected BAC clones <a href="#pone.0022728-Rounsley1" class="ref-tip">[49]</a>, <a href="#pone.0022728-Wicker1" class="ref-tip">[50]</a> and when using pyrosequencing as a supplement in capillary sequencing <a href="#pone.0022728-Velasco1" class="ref-tip">[51]</a>. The average lengths of unpaired reads were close to those obtained during sequencing of the rice genome <a href="#pone.0022728-Rounsley1" class="ref-tip">[49]</a>. In turn, the paired Titanium reads were approximately two times longer than those described for GS FLX paired end libraries <a href="#pone.0022728-Quinn1" class="ref-tip">[45]</a>, <a href="#pone.0022728-Miller1" class="ref-tip">[52]</a>. The share of paired fragments was similar to the share described in the earlier reports <a href="#pone.0022728-Quinn1" class="ref-tip">[45]</a>.</p>
</div>

<div id="section4" class="section toc-section"><a id="s2a4" name="s2a4" class="link-target" title="Genome assembly"></a><h4>Genome assembly.</h4><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec4.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec4.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>The results of both versions of genome assembly are presented in <a href="#pone-0022728-t001">Table 1</a> and <a href="#pone.0022728.s020">Table S3</a>. In <a href="#pone.0022728.s021">Table S4</a>, the characteristics are given for utilization of sequence reads by the indicated programs. The results obtained during assembly of the genome according to version A (Celera) and version B (Celera and Arachne) in obtaining the first version of the line B10 genome are described in more detail below:</p>


<ul class="bulleted">

<li>Version A – assembling the 8× UN and 4× PA reads with sequences of BAC ends using the Celera 5.4 program</li>

</ul><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec4.p2" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec4.p2" class="link-target"></a><p>The assembled pyrosequenced and BES reads provided 15,667 contigs with a combined length of 197 Mbp, average length of 12,972 bp and N50 of 27,086 bp. The number of supercontigs was 4,173 with a total coverage of 224 Mbp, average length 54,070 bp and N50 2,324,038 bp. Another result of the genome assembly was the identification of contigs of repetitive sequences. These degenerate contigs contain partly assembled repetitive sequences, which could not be uniquely assigned by the Celera program to the remaining sequences. There were 148,607 degenerate contigs with a joint length of over 72 Mbp and average size of 490 bp. In average they were made in 60% of the repetitive sequences.</p>


<ul class="bulleted">

<li>Version B stage 1 – Celera – assembling the 8× UN and 4× PA reads after 454 Titanium sequencing using the Celera 5.4 program</li>

</ul><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec4.p3" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec4.p3" class="link-target"></a><p>The result of the assembly of the reads after pyrosequencing was 15,514 contigs of 195.6 Mbp, with an average length of 12,839 bp, and N50 of 24,714 bp. The number of supercontigs was 4,976 with a total coverage of 197.6 Mbp, average length of 39,799 bp, and N50 of 158,310 bp. The number of degenerate contigs was 160,714 with an average length of 476 bp, jointly encompassing 76.5 Mbp from which, in average, over 61% were recognized as repetitive sequences.</p>


<ul class="bulleted">

<li>Version B stage 2 – Arachne – hybrid assembly of results from stage 1 with sequences of BAC ends and 1,781 additional cucumber sequences (see: <a href="#s4">Material &amp; Methods</a>) using the Arachne program.</li>

</ul><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec4.p4" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec4.p4" class="link-target"></a><p>As a result of hybrid assembly,16,547 contigs were obtained with a total length of 193.2 Mbp, an average of 12,214 bp and an N50 equal to 23,280 bp. The number of supercontigs was 13,129 with a total length of 323 Mbp, average coverage of 25,865 bp and N50 of 323,092 bp.</p>
<a class="link-target" id="pone-0022728-t001" name="pone-0022728-t001"></a><div class="figure" data-doi="10.1371/journal.pone.0022728.t001"><div class="img-box"><a title="Click for larger image" href="article/figure/image?size=medium&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0022728.t001" data-doi="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0022728" data-uri="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0022728.t001"><img src="article/figure/image?size=inline&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0022728.t001" alt="thumbnail" class="thumbnail" loading="lazy"></a><div class="expand"></div></div><div class="figure-inline-download">
          Download:

                </a></div><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=original&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0022728.t001">
                original image
              </a></li></ul></div><div class="figcaption"><span>Table 1. </span> Cucumber genome assembly statistics.</div><p class="caption_target"></p><p class="caption_object"><a href="https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0022728.t001">
              https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0022728.t001</a></p></div><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec4.p5" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec4.p5" class="link-target"></a><p>The sequence of the genome of the Borszczagowski cultivar (line B10) with the characteristics given in the second row of <a href="#pone-0022728-t001">Table 1</a> (Celera+Arachne, version B step 2) was deposited in GenBank under No. ACYN01000000 on September 21, 2009. It is also available, together with additional data (i.e. gene predictions, proteins, functional annotation) under the web address <a href="http://csgenome.sggw.pl">http://csgenome.sggw.pl</a> of the Polish Consortium of Cucumber Genome Sequencing.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec4.p6" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec4.p6" class="link-target"></a><p>For genome assembly, a hybrid method was selected which is often used for combining fragments generated by the classical Sanger technique and by next generation sequencing technologies <a href="#pone.0022728-Wicker1" class="ref-tip">[50]</a>, <a href="#pone.0022728-Goldberg1" class="ref-tip">[53]</a>. The detailed characteristics of reads used in genome assembly are provided in, <a href="#pone.0022728.s018">Tables S1</a> and <a href="#pone.0022728.s019">S2</a> and <a href="#pone.0022728.s004">Figures S1</a> A, B. The sequence unique contigs of B10 line genome assembled in each version (A (Celera) 197,5 Mbp and B (Celera/Arachne) 193 Mbp), is similar (<a href="#pone.0022728.s020">Table S3</a>) to both the result of the Chinese 9930 line assembly (185 Mbp) <a href="#pone.0022728-Huang1" class="ref-tip">[13]</a> and American Gy14 line (200 Mbp) (Miller et al. Shotgun Assembly of a Repetitive Plant Genome. Sequencing, Finishing &amp; Analysis in the Future. Meeting 27<sup>th</sup>–29th May 2009 Santa Fe, NM, USA 14 (2009)) <a href="#pone.0022728-Cavagnaro1" class="ref-tip">[54]</a>. In the work of Huang et al., 2009 <a href="#pone.0022728-Huang1" class="ref-tip">[13]</a>, the assembly was done using the SOAPdenovo software from mostly Illumina reads (68,3× coverage) together with “Sanger” generated genomic library end sequences (3,9× coverage). The American Gy14 line genome was assembled from 454 Titanium pyrosequenced reads: unpaired (24× coverage) and paired (12× coverage) (3 Kbp and 20 Kbp) using the Celera (Miller et al. Shotgun Assembly of a Repetitive Plant Genome. Sequencing, Finishing &amp; Analysis in the Future. Meeting 27th–29th May 2009 Santa Fe, NM, USA 14 (2009); <a href="http://sourceforge.net/apps/mediawiki/wgs-assembler/index.php?title=Cucumber_Poster">http://sourceforge.net/apps/mediawiki/wgs-assembler/index.php?title=Cucumber_Poster</a>) and the Newbler software <a href="#pone.0022728-Cavagnaro1" class="ref-tip">[54]</a> (<a href="http://www.phytozome.net/cucumber.php">http://www.phytozome.net/cucumber.php</a>). As seen in <a href="#pone.0022728.s020">Table S3</a>, the assembly of the B10 line genome using the Celera Assembler alone with the Titanium reads and BESs (version A), only slightly increased the coverage of supercontigs with respect to that obtained using solely pyrosequenced reads (version B step 1). The coverage was increased by 27 Mbp (224 Mbp obtained for the combination of reads vs. 198 Mbp using second generation reads alone). This may be due to the fact that the Celera assembler mapped only 66.54% of BAC ends on the sequence contigs (<a href="#pone.0022728.s021">Table S4</a>) and assigned the rest to “degenerate” repetitive sequences. The percentage of BESs contained in contigs made by the Arachne program (version B step 2) were 82.04%, from which 81.12% were placed on contigs resulted from next generation reads assembly alone (version B step 1). Comparison of both final assembly versions (A – Celera and B – Celera/Arachne), presented in <a href="#pone-0022728-t001">Table 1</a>, indicated that 190 Mbp of sequence showed a similarity ≥98% between assembled versions of the genome, whereas the share of sequences distinguishing them was 10 Mbp (5.12%). The difference is caused mainly by diverse repetitive sequences recognizing algorithms implemented in both assemblers <a href="#pone.0022728-Miller1" class="ref-tip">[52]</a>, <a href="#pone.0022728-Batzoglou1" class="ref-tip">[55]</a>. Those dissimilar number of used BESs together with the specific algorithms, could also be the reason of: a) differences in unique assembled contigs size (Celera: 197,5 Mbp vs. Celera/Arachne: 193 Mbp), b) the mentioned 10 Mbp of sequences dissimilar between both assemblies versions, c) significant differences of genome size in supercontigs (Celera: 224 Mbp vs. Celera/Arachne: 322 Mbp).</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec4.p7" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec4.p7" class="link-target"></a><p>Significantly diverse supercontigs' total length resulted mainly from differences of scafolded and nonscafolded contigs length as well as in length of supercontigs' gaps. Total length of real supercontigs (consisting of at least 2 contigs) in each assembly version is similar (Celera: 218 Mbp vs. Celera/Arachne: 228 Mbp), but Celera scaffolds have about 4.5-times less gaps (12%) and about 2-times more genomic sequences (88%). The Celera real supercontigs contain 97% of total contigs length while supercontigs after Celera/Arachne only 52%. Cross mapping of the chromosome anchored sequences of B (Celera/Arachne) and A (Celera) genomic sequences, demonstrated that about 79% of Celera/Arachne single scaffolds (containing only one contig) (ca. 73 Mbp), were located in gaps of its own real supercontigs (not shown). The above mentioned information suggest that the Celera/Arachne scafolded genome size, should probably sum up to 247 Mbp. It become clear that, in compare to the Arachne, Celera assembler made more compressed assembly by finding more connections between contigs, which resulted in less and longer supercontigs.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec4.p8" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec4.p8" class="link-target"></a><p>In order to assemble the sequences more precisely it appears necessary to first combine the results of the two assembled versions by creating a so-called “reconciliation assembly” <a href="#pone.0022728-Zimin1" class="ref-tip">[56]</a>. Of the two versions we have chosen the Celera/Arachne assembly (version B step 2) to provide the basic outline of the genome because of the larger (by about 15%) number of BAC ends mapped on sequence contigs (<a href="#pone.0022728.s021">Table S4</a>). Because of containing 2-times longer supercontigs, build (in average) from 2-times more contigs, the genome sequence of version A (Celera) was used to anchor more Arachne contigs (version B) (163 Mbp vs. 80 Mbp) to seven cucumber chromosomes. The proper assembly of the cucumber line B10 genome was confirmed by the assignment of 96.56% of 63035 purified Unigene ESTs (International Cucurbit Genomics Initiative (ICuGI), <a href="http://www.icugi.org/cgi-bin/ICuGI/EST/home.cgi?organism=cucumber">http://www.icugi.org/cgi-bin/ICuGI/EST/home.cgi?organism=cucumber</a>) and of 6 cucumber genomic sequence clones (BAC/Fosmid) of 372,277 bp with an average similarity of 98%. These values are comparable to those given for the assembly of the genome of line 9930. The genome coverage values do not differ from those reported for other species <a href="#pone.0022728-Eck1" class="ref-tip">[57]</a>, <a href="#pone.0022728-Zharkikh1" class="ref-tip">[58]</a>. Mapping of reads after pyrosequencing on assembled sequence contigs indicated that their average coverage is 14.20×, of which over 98% of the genome sequence length has a coverage of over 3×, and 95% is greater than 5× (<a href="#pone.0022728.s005">Fig. S2</a>). The resulted assembled genome coverage is sufficient to correctly recognize SNP and In/Del polymorphism <a href="#pone.0022728-Wicker1" class="ref-tip">[50]</a>, <a href="#pone.0022728-Eck1" class="ref-tip">[57]</a>, <a href="#pone.0022728-Walter1" class="ref-tip">[59]</a>–<a href="#pone.0022728-Wheat1" class="ref-tip">[61]</a>.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec4.p9" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec4.p9" class="link-target"></a><p>It is worth to note, that despite the use of all ranges of paired end libraries (2 Kbp–10 Kbp, 20 Kbp, 40 Kbp, 140 Kbp) in each of mentioned three cucumber genome projects (B10, 9930 and Gy14), the resulted scaffolds total lengths (<a href="#pone.0022728.s020">Table S3</a>) were only about 200 Mbp, what states for 54% of the flow cytometry estimated cucumber genome size of 367 Mbp <a href="#pone.0022728-Arumuganathan1" class="ref-tip">[12]</a>. The only aberrance is the Celera/Arachne B10 line assembly supercontigs length (322 Mbp – 88%), though, like it was concluded, it should probably sum up to 247 Mbp (67%). The assembled genome length of the other species' recently published sequencing projects using next generation sequencing (NGS) reads, were close to the flow cytometry's estimated genome sizes i.e. 95% wild strawberry (454, Illumina, Solid) <a href="#pone.0022728-Shulaev1" class="ref-tip">[62]</a>, 76% cocoa (454, Illumina) <a href="#pone.0022728-Argout1" class="ref-tip">[63]</a>, 85% turkey (454, Illumina) <a href="#pone.0022728-Dalloul1" class="ref-tip">[64]</a>. We suppose, that the reasons why the draft cucumber nuclear genome sequences, in all the three mentioned world-wide projects (Polish, Chinese, American), sum up to only ca. 200 Mbp, could be: a) specific distribution of repeated sequences and/or b) underrepresentation of genome in sequencing reads. Though the underrepresentation of genome sequence reads from both capillary and second generation sequencing technologies is well known <a href="#pone.0022728-Schatz1" class="ref-tip">[65]</a>, any confirmation of the proposed specific repeat content distribution would need additional in-deep studies. A this moment, we believe that the use of the long direct 3<sup>rd</sup> sequencing technologies' reads as well as sequencing of ends coming from very long (about 40 Kbp) paired end libraries (needed to jump through the long rRNA stretches <a href="#pone.0022728-Zhao1" class="ref-tip">[66]</a>) could facilitate the ongoing work to the finished cucumber genome sequence.</p>
</div>

<div id="section5" class="section toc-section"><a id="s2a5" name="s2a5" class="link-target" title="Characterization of the genome on the basis of BESs and assembled contigs sequences"></a><h4>Characterization of the genome on the basis of BESs and assembled contigs sequences.</h4><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec5.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec5.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>The preliminary characterization of the genome performed on BESs and genome contigs gave three values: the percentage content of GC pairs (36.60%), the occurrence of microsatellite repeats and plant repetitive elements.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec5.p2" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec5.p2" class="link-target"></a><p>The participation of the total length of microsatellite sequences (<a href="#pone.0022728.s022">Table S5</a>) was found to be 0.73% in BESs and 0.94% in contigs. Though for the comparison proposes the <a href="#pone.0022728.s022">Table S5</a> contains different measurements, like microsatellites' length, count, density and mean repeat number, to be the most objective we will report here on % of relative length of SSRs. The most abundant SSRs in BESs were single nucleotide repeats (25.91%), dinucleotides(20.15%), tertanucleotides (19.28%) and trinucleotide repeats (18.62%). The penta- to octa-nucleotide repeats constituted 16.04% of all SSRs. Contigs contained mostly, dinucleotides (24.97%), tetranucleotides (23.36%) and trinucleotides (22.15%). The penta- to octa-nucleotide repeats constituted 19.69% of all SSRs.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec5.p3" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec5.p3" class="link-target"></a><p>When comparing the B10 line genome assembly contigs' microsatellite content and distribution (count, density and mean repeat length (<a href="#pone.0022728.s022">Table S5</a>)) with the previous report by Cavagnaro et al. <a href="#pone.0022728-Cavagnaro1" class="ref-tip">[54]</a> done on Gy14 cucumber line genome, the results are almost identical. The slight difference in reporting content of di- to tetranucleotides is because of the analysis of mononucleotides in our results. When the mononucleotides were not taken into account the results were practically identical (not shown). The content of plant repetitive elements (<a href="#pone.0022728.s023">Table S6</a>) was evaluated as 48.13% in BESs and 17.82% in contigs. 23.42% of BESs repeats were similar to previously classified plant sequences whereas 23.81% were specific for cucumber (mainly satellite DNA type I, II, III, IV) <a href="#pone.0022728-Han1" class="ref-tip">[67]</a>. Known <em>versus</em> specific repeat portions in contigs were 7.73% and 10.09%, respectively. The most abundant group of known repetitive sequences were mobile elements (9.70% in BESs and 6.35% in contigs), ribosomal RNA genes (8.96% and 0.20%), retrotransposons (8.65% in BESs) and small RNA sequences (4.63% and 0.11%).</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec5.p4" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec5.p4" class="link-target"></a><p>The comparison of microsatellite content between BESs and contigs' sequences indicates more than 2.5-fold lower participation of mononucleotide repeats in contigs, whereas the share of other multinucleotide repeats is higher in assembled contigs. The content of plant repetitive elements in sequence contigs is on the average 3-fold lower than in BESs. The greatest differentiation of the participation of these elements concerns ribosomal RNA genes (45S and 5S) and small RNA sequences, of which there are respectively 48-, 14- and 42-fold fewer in sequence contigs. Genomic contigs mostly contain unique sequences. This is due to the specifics of automatic assembly algorithms of sequencing reads <a href="#pone.0022728-Farrer1" class="ref-tip">[68]</a>. Therefore, when the finished genome sequence is not available, the comparisons and characterization of the content of repetitive elements is more appropriate on the basis of long, not assembled reads, like the sequences of genomic libraries e.g. BESs</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec5.p5" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec5.p5" class="link-target"></a><p>The share of repetitive plant elements in the assembled line B10 genome is approx. 1.34 lower than their content in line 9930 contigs as described by Huang S. et al. 2009 <a href="#pone.0022728-Huang1" class="ref-tip">[13]</a>. This may be due to the additional number of contigs which constitute repetitive sequences in the public genome sequence of line 9930. The number of repetitive plant elements in unique sequences (183 Mbp) of the line 9930 genome determined using the database of repetitive cucumber elements created in this work for B10 line was 16.09% and did not differ from the repetitive element content of assembled B10 line genome.</p>
</div>

<div id="section6" class="section toc-section"><a id="s2a6" name="s2a6" class="link-target" title="Assignment of contigs to chromosomes"></a><h4>Assignment of contigs to chromosomes.</h4><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec6.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec6.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>From the set of 2051 markers of the cucumber genetic map (<a href="http://cucumber.genomics.org.cn">http://cucumber.genomics.org.cn</a>) <a href="#pone.0022728-Ren1" class="ref-tip">[69]</a> including 995 SSRs, 1,027 DArTs, and 29 SCARs/RAPDs, the available sequences of 1,883 markers (964 SSR and 919 DArT sequences) enabled the assignment of the assembled genome to specific chromosomes. The unique locations were obtained for 1,665 markers on the genome sequence after version B step 2 (Celera/Arachne) (<a href="#pone.0022728.s024">Table S7</a>), through which 80 Mbp of contigs sequences and 160 Mbp of pseudo-molecule scaffolds were assigned to chromosomes. The genome sequence in version A (Celera) allowed the assignment of 173 correctly placed and oriented pseudo-molecule scaffolds of 207 Mbp length (containing 182 Mbp in sequence contigs), through 1,700 uniquely mapped markers. It is worth to note, that because of the manual anchoring of the Celera scaffolds to the chromosomes using BLAST (instead of Arachne module for Celera/Arachne assembly), and the use of long sequence markers it was possible to choose only the Celera pseudo-molecule scaffolds which were not only placed but also oriented correctly.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec6.p2" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec6.p2" class="link-target"></a><p>The genome sequence obtained in version A, due to the presence of 2-fold longer scaffolds containing 2-fold additional contigs, was used to facilitate the assignment of a larger number of Celera/Arachne sequences (<a href="#pone.0022728.s024">Table S7</a>) to seven cucumber chromosomes. Subsequently as a result of the unique cross-alignment of sequences of both final assembly versions (<a href="#pone-0022728-t002">Table 2</a>), a total length of 163 Mbp of Celera/Arachne contigs as well as 167 Mbp of Celera contigs were finally assigned to chromosomes. That cross alignment with the correctly placed and oriented Celera pseudo-molecule scaffolds enabled also the correct placing and orientation of Arachne/Celera sequences. The above mentioned contigs were assigned through 9358 Celera/Arachne pseudo-molecule scaffolds (300 Mbp) and 169 Celera pseudo-molecule scaffolds (207 Mbp).</p>
<a class="link-target" id="pone-0022728-t002" name="pone-0022728-t002"></a><div class="figure" data-doi="10.1371/journal.pone.0022728.t002"><div class="img-box"><a title="Click for larger image" href="article/figure/image?size=medium&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0022728.t002" data-doi="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0022728" data-uri="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0022728.t002"><img src="article/figure/image?size=inline&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0022728.t002" alt="thumbnail" class="thumbnail" loading="lazy"></a><div class="expand"></div></div><div class="figure-inline-download">
          Download:
          <ul><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/powerpoint?id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0022728.t002">
                  PPT
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/powerpoint?id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0022728.t002">
                PowerPoint slide
              </a></li><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=large&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0022728.t002">
                  PNG
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=large&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0022728.t002">
                larger image
              </a></li><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=original&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0022728.t002">
                  TIFF
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=original&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0022728.t002">
                original image
              </a></li></ul></div><div class="figcaption"><span>Table 2. </span> The final result of joint anchoring of genome sequences to chromosomes<span class="xref"><sup>a</sup></span>.</div><p class="caption_target"></p><p class="caption_object"><a href="https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0022728.t002">
              https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0022728.t002</a></p></div><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec6.p3" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec6.p3" class="link-target"></a><p>Genomic contigs of the line B10 were finally assigned to chromosomes on the basis of 1,701 markers, which represents 3.6% fewer markers than used for line 9930 <a href="#pone.0022728-Huang1" class="ref-tip">[13]</a>. This difference may be due to the genetic distance between B10 line (European cucumber) and the individuals from the population used for mapping line 9930 (Asian cucumber) <a href="#pone.0022728-Huang1" class="ref-tip">[13]</a>. In spite of this difference, 2% and 4.5% more sequences of, respectively, Celera/Arachne and Celera contigs were assigned and the length of pseudo-molecule scaffolds mapped to seven chromosomes was 70% (version B step 2) and 17% (version A) greater than that of line 9930 <a href="#pone.0022728-Huang1" class="ref-tip">[13]</a>. The participation of a larger part of the line B10 genome placed on chromosomes, relative to line 9930, both with respect to contigs as well as pseudo-molecule scaffolds, is probably due to B10 having 2-fold longer scaffolds with a greater number of longer contigs and/or the use of other methods of sequencing and other algorithms of sequence assembly.</p>
</div>

<div id="section7" class="section toc-section"><a id="s2a7" name="s2a7" class="link-target" title="Gene prediction"></a><h4>Gene prediction.</h4><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec7.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec7.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>As a result of <em>ab initio</em> gene prediction using GeneMark-ES software <a href="#pone.0022728-Lomsadze1" class="ref-tip">[70]</a> on 15,678 genomic contigs with masked repetitive sequences, we obtained 26,587 gene models encoding proteins longer than 50 amino acids. The predicted gene structures (<a href="#pone.0022728.s025">Table S8</a>) contain an average of 5.49 exons each with an average length of 201 bp and introns with an average length of 436 bp. Intergenic sequences have an average length of 3,009 bp. The average length of the coding region is 1103 bp and the average length of the transcribed region is 3,058 bp. The average gene length (including introns) is 4,563 bp.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec7.p2" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec7.p2" class="link-target"></a><p>The sensitivity (Sn) and specificity (Sp) of the gene prediction method was determined on a test set (<a href="#pone.0022728.s026">Table S9</a>).</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec7.p3" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec7.p3" class="link-target"></a><p>We also used the GeneMark-ES models to predict genes in the genomic contigs of the Chinese Long cultivar (<a href="#pone.0022728.s025">Table S8</a>). For 12,195 contigs, we obtained 26,442 gene models encoding proteins longer than 50 amino acid residues. The predicted gene structures contain an average of 5.56 exons with an average length of 216 bp and introns with an average length of 434 bp. We observed the following average lengths for intergenic sequences (2,668 bp), coding regions (1,200 bp), transcribed regions (3,178 bp), and the entire genes including introns (4,512 bp).</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec7.p4" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec7.p4" class="link-target"></a><p>The average lengths of introns, exons and their numbers are similar for the line B10 and 9930 genomes when comparing gene predictions done in this project and as by Huang et al. 2009 <a href="#pone.0022728-Huang1" class="ref-tip">[13]</a> (<a href="#pone.0022728.s025">Table S8</a>), however slight differences can be also observed. Differences are with respect to the number of exons and introns per gene, the length of the intergenic region, and the average length of the translated and transcribed regions. These slight differences between predictions made in this work for genomes of lines 9930 and B10 and the results of Huang S. et al. 2009 <a href="#pone.0022728-Huang1" class="ref-tip">[13]</a> could be caused by the different methodologies used for structural annotation and/or genome assembly, as well as the presence of considerable bacterial DNA impurities in line 9930 (<a href="#pone.0022728.s025">Table S8</a>, and <a href="#pone.0022728.s006">Fig. S3</a> and <a href="#pone.0022728.s007">S4</a>).</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec7.p5" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec7.p5" class="link-target"></a><p>In an analysis using the test-set, high values were obtained for sensitivity and specificity of the prediction model (<a href="#pone.0022728.s026">Table S9</a>). These values were similar to those obtained for other eukaryotic organisms, both using demanding programs (for generating a model) and the training set with a large number of cDNA/EST <a href="#pone.0022728-Haas1" class="ref-tip">[71]</a>, <a href="#pone.0022728-Wei1" class="ref-tip">[72]</a> coding sequences as well as using the GeneMark.hmm ES program <a href="#pone.0022728-Lomsadze1" class="ref-tip">[70]</a>, <a href="#pone.0022728-TerHovhannisyan1" class="ref-tip">[73]</a>.</p>
</div>

<div id="section8" class="section toc-section"><a id="s2a8" name="s2a8" class="link-target" title="Functional annotation"></a><h4>Functional annotation.</h4><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec8.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec8.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>Among the 23,190 proposed proteins of line B10 which are longer than 100 amino acid residues, similarities in the GenBank database identified 19,562 of them and 16,944 had informative names (<a href="#pone.0022728.s027">Table S10</a>). A total of 12,463 of the proteins could be assigned to functional groups. In the main Gene Ontology Consortium <a href="#pone.0022728-Ashburner1" class="ref-tip">[74]</a> classification, the following numbers of functional subgroups were identified: 923 BP (Biological Process), 1244 MF (Molecular Function), and 247 CC (Cellular Compoment). In line 9930, out of 23,480 proteins longer than 100 amino acid residues, similarities were identified for 20,361 predictions. Among these there were 17,937 described proteins, and 13,485 of these were assigned to functional groups (<a href="#pone.0022728.s027">Table S10</a>). In the detailed Gene Ontology Consortium <a href="#pone.0022728-Ashburner1" class="ref-tip">[74]</a> classification, the following numbers of functional subgroups were distinguished: 1,023 BP, 1,412 MF, and 266 CC.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec8.p2" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec1.sec8.p2" class="link-target"></a><p>The analysis of functional groups indicated that 1,500 proteins from line 9930 (in contrast to only 5 from line B10) had functions specific for bacterial proteins such as: chemotaxis, ciliary or flagellar mobility, enterobacterial common antigen biosynthetic process, flagellum assembly, flagellum organization, gram-negative-bacterium-type cell wall and pilus, spore germination. After careful analysis of the sequences of contigs containing these proteins, 554 contigs (average GC pair content 55.54%) with a high similarity (approx. 85%) to bacterial genome sequences (<a href="#pone.0022728.s006">Fig. S3</a>) were isolated and removed from subsequent analyses. The characteristics of genome sequences before and after removal of sequences representing bacterial contamination are presented in <a href="#pone.0022728.s025">Table S8</a> and the updated functional statistics of proteins for the purified set of contigs of line 9930 are shown in <a href="#pone.0022728.s027">Table S10</a>. <a href="#pone.0022728.s007">Figure S4</a> indicates the participation of individual bacterial genera to which the sequences of the assembled contigs of line 9930 (GenBank database under No. ACHR01000000) show similarity.</p>
</div>
</div>

<div id="section2" class="section toc-section"><a id="s2b" name="s2b" class="link-target" title="Whole genome comparisons between European and Asian cucumber cultivars"></a>
<h3>Whole genome comparisons between European and Asian cucumber cultivars</h3>

<div id="section1" class="section toc-section"><a id="s2b1" name="s2b1" class="link-target" title="Analysis of SNPs and INDELs"></a><h4>Analysis of SNPs and INDELs.</h4><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec2.sec1.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec2.sec1.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>The alignment of genomic contigs of the cucumber cultivars is presented in <a href="#pone.0022728.s008">Figure S5</a>. Comparative analysis of unique mapped contigs longer than 1000 bp of the European and Chinese lines, 13,428 (187.8 Mbp) and 11,219 (181.8 Mbp), respectively, showed 97.40% similarity of the assembled sequences.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec2.sec1.p2" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec2.sec1.p2" class="link-target"></a><p>The characteristics of SNP and INDEL polymorphism between compared cucumber genomes are presented in <a href="#pone.0022728.s028">Table S11</a>. SNP polymorphism was generally 2 times higher than INDELs. Uncategorized whole genomic sequences polymorphism frequency was 1/237 bp for SNPs and 1/395 bp for INDELs. It was found that frequency of SNPs and INDELs in exons (1/626 bp (SNP) and 1/1724 bp (INDEL)) is in average 2-times lower than in genes (introns and exons together) (1/403 bp and 1/729 bp) and more than 4-times lower than found in whole genomic contigs sequences. Regions of gene's promoters (−1000 bp upstream translation start), showed polymorphism level (1/260 bp and 1/417 bp) similar to that of uncategorized whole genomic sequences. Beside exons, where the ratio of insertions and deletions is equal, all other parts of the genome sequences have 2-times lower frequency of insertions then deletions in B10 line.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec2.sec1.p3" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec2.sec1.p3" class="link-target"></a><p>Analysis of INDELs' length distribution (1–2 bp (ultra), 1–10 bp (micro), 11–50 bp (mini), 51–100 bp (midi) – <a href="#pone.0022728.s029">Table S12</a>), showed that in uncategorized whole genome sequences as well as in noncoding promoter regions of B10 line, almost 60% of all insertions and 85% of all deletions are ultrashort (1–2 bp) (INDEL polymorphism will be counted according to the B10 line). Insertions and deletions of micro regions (3–10 bp) taken 25% and 10%, and of mini regions (11–50 bp) taken 15% and 3%, respectively. The longest (midi regions: &gt;51 bp) insertions and deletions, taken 1% and 0.1%, respectively. Unlike above mentioned sequences, exons have almost equal amounts of insertions and deletions (average share of 51% ultra-, 27% micro and 16% mini- INDELs). The slight difference of share were found only for the midi- INDELs. There were 5% of insertions and 8% of deletions. It was also found that exons have 12-times higher frequency of midi-deletions (&gt;51 bp), more than 8-times lower frequency of ultra-deletions and 4-times lower frequency of ultra-insertions than in the rest of the genome sequences.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec2.sec1.p4" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec2.sec1.p4" class="link-target"></a><p>SNP polymorphism frequency found between two cucumber lines (4.22/1 Kbp), is slightly lower than the one of Arabidopsis thaliana ecotypes (4.68/1 Kbp) <a href="#pone.0022728-Ossowski1" class="ref-tip">[60]</a>, <a href="#pone.0022728-Schmid1" class="ref-tip">[75]</a> and almost 3-times lower than of <em>Oryza sativa</em> indica and japonica varieties (31.74/1 Kpz) <a href="#pone.0022728-Yu1" class="ref-tip">[76]</a>. The SNP polymorphism frequency in cucumber coding sequences (exons) (1.60/1 Kbp), is almost 2-times lower than in <em>Arabidopsis</em> (2,79/1 Kbp) and rice (3/1 Kbp). The cucumber promotor regions are also slightly less polymorphic (3.85/1 Kbp) than in rice (4.61/1 Kbp). The cucumber INDEL polymorphism frequency (&lt;100 bp) found in uncategorized whole genome sequences (2.53/1 Kbp) was almost identical to that of rice (2.66/1 Kbp), but almost 4-times higher than in <em>Arabidopsis</em> (0.65/1 Kbp). The differences found in exons are more significant. There were above 2.5-times more insertions and deletions found in cucumber (0.58/1 Kbp) than in rice (0.22/1 Kbp) and 29-times more than in <em>Arabidopsis</em>. The INDELs polymorphism frequency found in cucumber promotor regions was almost 2-times higher (2.4/1 Kbp) than in rice (1.28/1 Kbp). The rates of polymorphism found in those three species, seems to mirror the geographical and climate diversity between compared genotypes, their genetic background as well as the share of repeated sequences in the genomes – which are the most variable genome regions.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec2.sec1.p5" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec2.sec1.p5" class="link-target"></a><p>The most frequent type of sequencing error generated by pyrosequencing introduce artificial insertions or deletions of single nucleotides. This type of error arise when sequencer reads the genome region containing single nucleotides repeats (homopolymer runs) <a href="#pone.0022728-Wicker1" class="ref-tip">[50]</a>, <a href="#pone.0022728-Wheat1" class="ref-tip">[61]</a>, <a href="#pone.0022728-Margulies1" class="ref-tip">[77]</a>. Those artificial INDELs could cause open reading frame shifts which then affect gene predictions and effect in functional annotation's artifacts <a href="#pone.0022728-Antonov1" class="ref-tip">[78]</a>, <a href="#pone.0022728-Kislyuk1" class="ref-tip">[79]</a>. The assembled genome's pyrosequencing reads coverage of 14× is considered to be sufficient for automatic (made during genome assembly) corrections of mentioned above homopolymer errors <a href="#pone.0022728-Wicker1" class="ref-tip">[50]</a>, <a href="#pone.0022728-Wheat1" class="ref-tip">[61]</a>. The more direct evidence of assembled sequence correctness is that ultra short (1–2 bp) INDELs (those which cause open reading frames shift) frequency was 6-times lower in exons than in other parts of the genome.</p>
</div>

<div id="section2" class="section toc-section"><a id="s2b2" name="s2b2" class="link-target" title="Analysis of genomic rearrangements"></a><h4>Analysis of genomic rearrangements.</h4><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec2.sec2.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec2.sec2.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>Besides of the single nucleotide polymorphism, another element of genetic differentiation of lines B10 and 9930 is represented by genomic rearrangements of smaller or larger DNA fragments. Such changes include inversions and translocations, as well as deletions/insertions and duplications, which have accumulated over many generations and enabled the plants for better adaptation to new growth conditions. This has been demonstrated for primitive organisms as well as higher plants <a href="#pone.0022728-Yogeeswaran1" class="ref-tip">[16]</a>–<a href="#pone.0022728-Colson1" class="ref-tip">[19]</a>. Most rearrangements observed between genotypes of two cucumber cultivars are of the inversion/translocation type (<a href="#pone-0022728-g001">Fig. 1</a> (schema) and <a href="#pone.0022728.s009">Fig. S6</a> (detailed comparison)). The translocations and inversions affect all chromosome and involve segments even as large as 5 Mbp. Translocations are mostly between the inner chromosome regions and the ends of the chromosome arms, so it was probably the way to make some “desired” genes more transcriptionaly active than other “not needed” genes. These rearrangements may contribute to the differentiation of the two cucumber lines and thus could be a novel aspect of the evolutionary adaptation mechanism.</p>
<a class="link-target" id="pone-0022728-g001" name="pone-0022728-g001"></a><div class="figure" data-doi="10.1371/journal.pone.0022728.g001"><div class="img-box"><a title="Click for larger image" href="article/figure/image?size=medium&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0022728.g001" data-doi="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0022728" data-uri="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0022728.g001"><img src="article/figure/image?size=inline&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0022728.g001" alt="thumbnail" class="thumbnail" loading="lazy"></a><div class="expand"></div></div><div class="figure-inline-download">

<div id="section3" class="section toc-section"><a id="s2b3" name="s2b3" class="link-target" title="Analysis of functional groups differences"></a><h4>Analysis of functional groups differences.</h4><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec2.sec3.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec2.sec3.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>The above structural differences can lead to changes in gene quantity between analyzed cucumber cultivars. To check if it is true, the functional annotation results were compared between cultivars. As shown in <a href="#pone.0022728.s010">Figure S7A</a>–C and in <a href="#pone.0022728.s003">Dataset S3</a>, the intra-species differences between both lines as well as between species are minimal. In deep comparative functional analysis were done for 38 functional groups (16 BP, 18 MF, 4 CC), which show at least 2-fold higher number of genes in one of the two genotypes. Those functional groups in B10 and 9930 lines, contained 126 (ca. 1% of GO genes) and 96 genes (0.78% GO genes), respectively. The analysis indicates (<a href="#pone-0022728-t003">Table 3</a> and <a href="#pone.0022728.s030">Table S13</a>) that in line B10, there is a larger number of genes involved in photosynthesis, sugar metabolism, respiration, regulation of gene expression and chlorophyll degradation, as well as more genes involved in assimilation of nitrogen in the form of ammonium ions. The Asian cucumber variety, on the other hand, is characterized by having more genes involved in regulation of tolerance to photo-oxidative stress and high temperatures.</p>
<a class="link-target" id="pone-0022728-t003" name="pone-0022728-t003"></a><div class="figure" data-doi="10.1371/journal.pone.0022728.t003"><div class="img-box"><a title="Click for larger image" href="article/figure/image?size=medium&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0022728.t003" data-doi="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0022728" data-uri="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0022728.t003"><img src="article/figure/image?size=inline&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0022728.t003" alt="thumbnail" class="thumbnail" loading="lazy"></a><div class="expand"></div></div><div class="figure-inline-download">

<div id="section3" class="section toc-section"><a id="s2c" name="s2c" class="link-target" title="Comparative analysis of gene promoters between species"></a>
<h3>Comparative analysis of gene promoters between species</h3>

<div id="section1" class="section toc-section"><a id="s2c1" name="s2c1" class="link-target" title="Distribution of ABREs, DREs and EREs in promoters of genes of C. sativus line B10"></a><h4>Distribution of ABREs, DREs and EREs in promoters of genes of <em>C. sativus</em> line B10.</h4><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec3.sec1.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec3.sec1.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>A total of 20,745 promoter sequences (−1,000 bp from the ATG start codon) of genes of <em>C. sativus</em> line B10 were analyzed with respect to the occurrence and distribution of ABREs, DREs and EREs. It was found that 1,294 ABREs, 4,118 DREs and 449 EREs occur in 1158, 3500 and 438 gene promoters, respectively. Statistical analysis indicated an enriched content of the analyzed CREs in gene promoters of <em>C. sativus</em> line B10 (<a href="#pone.0022728.s031">Table S14</a>). The enrichment factors for ABRE, DRE and ERE are 1.27, 0.95 and 2.09, respectively. Promoter sequences of the analyzed genes were divided into 50 nucleotide fragments. In each fragment, the content of the analyzed CREs was calculated. An increased density of the analyzed fragments was identified at a distance of about 300 bp from the start codon ATG (<a href="#pone.0022728.s011">Fig. S8</a>).</p>
</div>

<div id="section2" class="section toc-section"><a id="s2c2" name="s2c2" class="link-target" title="Relative content of ABREs, DREs and EREs in promoters of genes of A. thaliana, P. trichocarpa, O. sativa and two C. sativus lines: B10, 9930"></a><h4>Relative content of ABREs, DREs and EREs in promoters of genes of <em>A. thaliana</em>, <em>P. trichocarpa</em>, <em>O. sativa</em> and two <em>C. sativus</em> lines: B10, 9930.</h4><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec3.sec2.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec3.sec2.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>The occurrence of specific CREs and combinations thereof in gene promoters may explain the putative correlation between these elements and the specific plant responses to biotic and abiotic stress and as such, will determine the Darwinian fitness of a plant under chilling or freezing or drought conditions. For this reason we identified all genes in <em>A. thaliana</em>, <em>P. trichocarpa</em>, <em>O. sativa</em> and in two <em>C. sativus</em> lines: B10 and 9930, whose promoter sequences contain specific CREs (for detailed analysis of CREs in <em>C. sativus</em> line B10 promoters see <a href="#pone.0022728.s031">Table S14</a> and <a href="#pone.0022728.s011">Fig. S8</a>). We calculated the relative numbers of ABREs, DREs and EREs <em>per</em> genome (<a href="#pone-0022728-t004">Table 4</a>). The relative percent content of each of the analyzed CREs was calculated according to the formula = ((ABRE+DRE+ERE)/CRE)×100. In the promoters of all identified genes of the analyzed plant species, the occurrence of the DRE element is approximately 70%. The content of the ABRE element in the promoters of genes from <em>A. thaliana</em>, <em>C. sativus</em> and <em>P. trichocarpa</em> is similar and is approximately 22%, whereas in <em>O. sativa</em> it is only 8%. The number of ERE elements in the promoters of <em>A. thaliana</em>, <em>C. sativus</em> and <em>P. trichocarpa</em> genes is approx. 8%, whereas in <em>O. sativa</em> the content of this CRE is 18% (<a href="#pone-0022728-t004">Table 4</a>).</p>
<a class="link-target" id="pone-0022728-t004" name="pone-0022728-t004"></a><div class="figure" data-doi="10.1371/journal.pone.0022728.t004"><div class="img-box"><a title="Click for larger image" href="article/figure/image?size=medium&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0022728.t004" data-doi="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0022728" data-uri="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0022728.t004"><img src="article/figure/image?size=inline&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0022728.t004" alt="thumbnail" class="thumbnail" loading="lazy"></a><div class="expand"></div></div><div class="figure-inline-download">
          Download:
          <ul><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/powerpoint?id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0022728.t004">
                  PPT
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/powerpoint?id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0022728.t004">
                PowerPoint slide
              </a></li><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=large&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0022728.t004">
                  PNG
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=large&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0022728.t004">
                larger image
              </a></li><li><div class="definition-label"><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=original&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0022728.t004">
                  TIFF
                </a></div><a href="article/figure/image?download&amp;size=original&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0022728.t004">
                original image
              </a></li></ul></div><div class="figcaption"><span>Table 4. </span> Relative content of ABREs, DREs and EREs in promoters with CREs in <em>A. thaliana</em>, <em>O. sativa</em>, <em>P. trichocarpa</em>, and <em>C. sativus</em> lines B10 and 9930.</div><p class="caption_target"></p><p class="caption_object"><a href="https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0022728.t004">
              https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0022728.t004</a></p></div><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec3.sec2.p2" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec3.sec2.p2" class="link-target"></a><p>The above results indicate that <em>A. thaliana</em> plants which are capable of overwintering in the vegetative form (as a green rosette), have the highest relative number of ABREs in their promoters and the lowest numbers of DREs and EREs relative to the remaining analyzed species and lines. The situation is different in <em>C. sativus</em> and <em>P. trichocarpa</em>. These plants do not overwinter like cucumbers, nor do they overwinter without leaves, like poplar trees. In the genome of <em>O. sativa</em>, a subtropical plant, which is not adapted for growth in ecosystem with chilling or freezing temperatures relative ABRE elements content is the lowest among all analyzed species. <em>O. sativa</em> is however adapted to others stresses such as root hypoxia and associated with it water uptake problems. Ethylene and ROS homeostasis in plant cells is controlled by a number of genes that regulate formation of lysigenous aerenchyma, light acclimatization and defense responses <a href="#pone.0022728-Muhlenbock1" class="ref-tip">[21]</a>, probably therefore has the highest number of ERE and DRE and the lowest ABRE elements in its genome. However orthologous groups of genes encoding <em>trans</em>-elements that interact with these CRE are highly conserved <a href="#pone.0022728-PrezRodrguez1" class="ref-tip">[81]</a> (<a href="#pone.0022728.s014">Fig. S11</a>, <a href="#pone.0022728.s015">S12</a>, <a href="#pone.0022728.s016">S13</a>, <a href="#pone.0022728.s017">S14</a>). The comparison of the occurrences of the three analyzed CREs in the promoters of two cucumber lines also showed significant differences. Promoters of genes in line 9930 are characterized by increased ABRE and ERE content and decreased DRE content, relative to the B10 cucumber line.</p>
</div>

<div id="section3" class="section toc-section"><a id="s2c3" name="s2c3" class="link-target" title="Functional classification of A. thaliana, P. trichocarpa, O. sativa, and C. sativus line B10 genes containing ABREs, DREs and EREs in their promoters"></a><h4>Functional classification of <em>A. thaliana</em>, <em>P. trichocarpa</em>, <em>O. sativa</em>, and <em>C. sativus</em> line B10 genes containing ABREs, DREs and EREs in their promoters.</h4><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec3.sec3.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec3.sec3.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>Genes of the analyzed species were classified according to function and cellular localization, into appropriate gene ontology (GO) groups: GO Cellular Component, GO Molecular Function and GO Biological Process. In the promoter sequences of the genes of each group, the occurrences of ABREs, DREs, EREs and the combinations ABRE+DRE, ABRE+ERE, DRE+ERE, and ABRE+DRE+ERE were determined for <em>A. thaliana</em>, <em>O. sativa</em>, <em>P. trichocarpa</em>, and <em>C. sativus</em> line B10. The analysis indicated differences in the number of chloroplast genes among the analyzed species (<a href="#pone.0022728.s012">Fig. S9C</a>). Genes belonging to this group are the most numerous in <em>A. thaliana</em> and <em>P. trichocarpa</em> and constitute approximately 10% of the pool of all analyzed genes for these species. Among the <em>A. thaliana</em> chloroplast genes, a significant increase was observed for the combinations ABRE+ERE (14%) and ABRE+DRE+ERE (16%) in their promoter sequences. The number of genes encoding proteins linked to the cell wall is decreased in <em>C. sativus</em> (0.5%), relative to genes of this class in the other species investigated (2–2.8%). In all analyzed species a preponderance of genes encoding proteins with hydrolase activity, which contain the combinations ABRE+ERE+DRE, DRE+ERE and ABRE+ERE in their promoters was observed (<a href="#pone.0022728.s012">Fig. S9B</a>). In <em>C. sativus</em>, on the other hand, we identified an increase in the number of genes encoding proteins with kinase activity, which contain the combinations ABRE+DRE+ERE and ABRE+DRE in their promoters.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec3.sec3.p2" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec3.sec3.p2" class="link-target"></a><p>The analysis of <em>C. sativus</em> line B10 genes linked to signal transduction indicated a distinct preponderance of genes containing the combination ABRE+DRE+ERE (7.6%) in their promoter sequences with respect to genes represented in this category in the remaining species (<a href="#pone.0022728.s012">Fig. S9A</a>). On the other hand, considerably fewer genes (approximately 2%) were identified as being linked to stress responses in line B10. Genes belonging to this class are the most highly represented in the three remaining species. <em>P. trichocarpa</em> contains EREs in 11.8% of its promoters, and the combination ABRE+ERE in 11.7% of its promoters. <em>O. sativa</em> contains ABREs in 8.7% of its promoters, the combination ABRE+DRE in 7.9% of its promoters, and the combination ABRE+ERE in 8.6% of its promoters. <em>A. thaliana</em> contains the combination ABRE+ERE in 8.3% of its promoters, and the combination ABRE+DRE+ERE in 8.5% of its promoters.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec3.sec3.p3" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec3.sec3.p3" class="link-target"></a><p>Among the genes involved in plant responses to biotic and abiotic factors, the most common combinations of elements in the promoters were ABRE+ERE in <em>A. thaliana</em> (8.6%) and in <em>O. sativa</em> (6.9%), ABRE+DRE+ERE (7.6%) in <em>C. sativus</em>, and ABRE+DRE (5,1%) in <em>P. trichocarpa</em>.</p>
</div>

<div id="section4" class="section toc-section"><a id="s2c4" name="s2c4" class="link-target" title="Functional analysis of genes containing ABREs, DREs and EREs in their promoters"></a><h4>Functional analysis of genes containing ABREs, DREs and EREs in their promoters.</h4><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec3.sec4.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec3.sec4.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>In order to determine which groups of genes in different species and lineages are regulated by the same CREs, and the same combinations of CREs and to analyze evolutionary changes in promoter sequences, we identified 5971 groups of orthologous genes from <em>A. thaliana</em>, <em>P. trichocarpa</em>, <em>O. sativa</em> and <em>C. sativus</em> line B10 and 9930 (<a href="#pone.0022728.s001">Dataset S1</a>) and we compared the genes which contain ABRE, DRE, ERE elements, the following combinations of two elements: ABRE+DRE; ABRE+ERE; and DRE+ERE in above mentioned species and lineages (<a href="#pone-0022728-g002">Fig. 2A</a> and <a href="#pone.0022728.s001">Dataset S1</a>). The functional classification of genes with above mentioned combinations of CREs is provided in <a href="#pone.0022728.s012">Fig. S9</a>.</p>
<a class="link-target" id="pone-0022728-g002" name="pone-0022728-g002"></a><div class="figure" data-doi="10.1371/journal.pone.0022728.g002"><div class="img-box"><a title="Click for larger image" href="article/figure/image?size=medium&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0022728.g002" data-doi="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0022728" data-uri="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0022728.g002"><img src="article/figure/image?size=inline&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0022728.g002" alt="thumbnail" class="thumbnail" loading="lazy"></a><div class="expand"></div></div><div class="figure-inline-download">
