C – control non-treated plants; ABA – abscisic acid treated plants.</p>
</p><p class="caption_object"><a href="https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0022728.g002">
              https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0022728.g002</a></p></div><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec3.sec4.p2" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec3.sec4.p2" class="link-target"></a><p>We identified 429, 420, 511, 361 and 738 groups of orthologous genes with promoters containing ABREs in <em>C. sativus</em> line B10, <em>C. sativus</em> line 9930, <em>A. thaliana</em>, <em>P. trichocarpa</em> and <em>O. sativa</em>, respectively. For DREs, we identified 1,164; 1,172; 1,835; 1,528 and 3,104 groups of orthologous genes with promoters containing these CREs in <em>C. sativus</em> line B10, <em>C. sativus</em> line 9930, <em>A. thaliana</em>, <em>P. trichocarpa</em> and <em>O. sativa</em>, respectively. For EREs, we identified 153, 148, 240, 242 and 1,214 groups of orthologous genes with promoters containing these CREs in <em>C. sativus</em> line B10, <em>C. sativus</em> line 9930, <em>A. thaliana</em>, <em>P. trichocarpa</em> and <em>O. sativa</em>, respectively. For the combination of elements ABRE+DRE, we identified 106, 98, 267, 141 and 398 groups of orthologous genes with promoters containing these CREs in <em>C. sativus</em> line B10, <em>C. sativus</em> line 9930, <em>A. thaliana</em>, <em>P. trichocarpa</em> and <em>O. sativa</em>, respectively. For the DRE+ERE combination, we identified 39, 37, 80, 48 and 699 groups of orthologous genes with promoters containing these CREs in <em>C. sativus</em> line B10, <em>C. sativus</em> line 9930, <em>A. thaliana</em>, <em>P. trichocarpa</em> and <em>O. sativa</em>, respectively. For the combination of elements ABRE+ERE, we identified 14, 16, 28, 19 and 136 groups of orthologous genes with promoters containing these CREs in <em>C. sativus</em> line B10, <em>C. sativus</em> line 9930, <em>A. thaliana</em>, <em>P. trichocarpa</em> and <em>O. sativa</em>, respectively.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec3.sec4.p3" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec3.sec4.p3" class="link-target"></a><p>Among the analyzed promoters of groups of orthologous genes containing ABREs in <em>A. thaliana</em>, <em>P. trichocarpa O. sativa</em> and two lines of <em>C. sativus</em>: B10 and 9930, only 13 were found to be common in these plant species (<a href="#pone-0022728-g002">Fig. 2A</a>). 95 groups of orthologous genes with promoters containing DREs were conserved in all analyzed species and lineages. In <em>A. thaliana</em>, <em>P. trichocarpa O. sativa</em> and two lines of <em>C. sativus</em>: B10 and 9930, among groups of orthologous genes containing ABRE+DRE combination in their promoters, 2 were common for the analyzed species (<a href="#pone-0022728-g002">Fig. 2A</a>, <a href="#pone.0022728.s001">Dataset S1</a>). These genes encode the following proteins: putative late embryogenesis abundant (LEA) and putative protein phosphatase 2C We have not identified any conserved groups of orthologous genes with promoters containing EREs and the following combinations of two elements: ABRE+ERE; and DRE+ERE in above mentioned species and lineages (<a href="#pone-0022728-g002">Fig. 2A</a>, <a href="#pone.0022728.s001">Dataset S1</a>).</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec3.sec4.p4" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec3.sec4.p4" class="link-target"></a><p>The analysis of the frequency of occurrence of mutations between genes (introns and exons) and their promoters within the two analyzed cucumber lines has indicated that both SNPs, as well as INDELs occur approximately 3 times more frequently in promoter regions than in gene sequences (<a href="#pone-0022728-t005">Table 5</a>). Promoters of selected orthologous genes of two <em>C. sativus</em> lines were compared using PlantCARE program <a href="#pone.0022728-Lescot1" class="ref-tip">[82]</a> and significant changes were observed in promoter sequences, which changed CRE combinations (<a href="#pone.0022728.s013">Fig. S10</a>).</p>
<a class="link-target" id="pone-0022728-t005" name="pone-0022728-t005"></a><div class="figure" data-doi="10.1371/journal.pone.0022728.t005"><div class="img-box"><a title="Click for larger image" href="article/figure/image?size=medium&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0022728.t005" data-doi="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0022728" data-uri="info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0022728.t005"><img src="article/figure/image?size=inline&amp;id=info:doi/10.1371/journal.pone.0022728.t005" alt="thumbnail" class="thumbnail" loading="lazy"></a><div class="expand"></div></div><div class="figure-inline-download">

              https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0022728.t005</a></p></div><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec3.sec4.p5" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec3.sec4.p5" class="link-target"></a><p>In order to confirm <em>in silico</em> prediction of different regulatory responses in different plant species to the same hormone we perform experiments with abcisic acid (<a href="#pone-0022728-g002">Fig. 2B</a>). Non-acclimated <em>Arabidopsis</em> and cucumber seedlings growing in ambient laboratory conditions were treated with 200 µM abscisic acid solution for 3 days and tested for freezing tolerance. Ion leakage after freezing to −2 or −4°C was significantly lower in <em>Arabidopsis</em> than in cucumber seedlings (<a href="#pone-0022728-g002">Fig. 2B</a>).</p>
</div>

<div id="section5" class="section toc-section"><a id="s2c5" name="s2c5" class="link-target" title="Characterization of C. sativus proteins linked to abiotic stress response"></a><h4>Characterization of <em>C. sativus</em> proteins linked to abiotic stress response.</h4><a id="article1.body1.sec2.sec3.sec5.p1" name="article1.body1.sec2.sec3.sec5.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>We analyzed genes encoding proteins from the LEA group (<strong><em>L</em></strong><em>ate </em><strong><em>E</em></strong><em>mbryogenesis </em><strong><em>A</em></strong><em>bundant</em>). In <em>C. sativus</em> line B10, only 26 genes of this type are present. For comparison there are 51 genes of this class in <em>A. thaliana</em> <a href="#pone.0022728-Hundertmark1" class="ref-tip">[83]</a> and 28 in <em>P. trichocarpa</em>. In <em>C. sativus</em> line B10, 9 of the LEA genes (34.6%) had ABREs and 8 had DREs (30.8%) in their promoter sequences. In <em>A. thaliana</em>, 32 genes of this class had ABREs (56.1%) and 34 had DREs (59.6%) in their promoter sequences. In <em>P. trichocarpa</em> 12 genes of this class had ABREs (42.9%) and 10 had DREs (35.7%) in their promoter sequences. These results may suggest that the decreased tolerance of cucumber to unfavorable environmental conditions (e.g. cold temperatures and water deficiency) is linked not only to the low number of LEA genes but also has a statistical effect on the relatively reduced presence of ABREs and DREs in their promoters. Cucumber sensitivity to stress probably also causes a reduction in the number of genes encoding scavengers or reactive oxygen species. In line B10 there are 116 such genes, whereas <em>A. thaliana</em> has 148. Only 3 groups of genes were present in similar numbers in <em>C. sativus</em> line B10 and <em>A. thaliana</em>. Among these genes are the monodehydroascorbate reductase genes, glutathione reductases, and catalases. The remaining groups (superoxide dismutases, ascorbate peroxidases, dehydroascorbate reductases, glutathione peroxidases, NADPH oxidases, peroxiredoxins, thioredoxins and glutaredoxins, were represented by a smaller number of genes in cucumber than in <em>A. thaliana</em>. We also described putative transcription factors representing AP2_EREBP and bZIP families from <em>C. sativus</em> line B10 and <em>C. sativus</em> line 9930, respectively (<a href="#pone.0022728.s002">Dataset S2</a>). We identified putative representatives of AREBs (Abscisic Acid–Responsive Element Binding Protein), ERFs (Ethylene-responsive Element-binding Factor) and DREBs/CBFs (Dehydration-responsive Element-binding proteins) (<a href="#pone.0022728.s014">Fig. S11</a>, <a href="#pone.0022728.s015">S12</a>, <a href="#pone.0022728.s016">S13</a>, <a href="#pone.0022728.s017">S14</a>).</p>
</div>
</div>
</div>

<div id="section3" class="section toc-section"><a id="s3" name="s3" data-toc="s3" class="link-target" title="Discussion"></a><h2>Discussion</h2><a id="article1.body1.sec3.p1" name="article1.body1.sec3.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>The draft genomic sequence of the North-European Borszczagowski cultivar of the cucumber <em>C. sativus</em> (line B10), provided herein, and the comparative genomics studies presented, has brought not only applied genomic research of cucumber but mainly basic research on plants to a new level. A quality draft of a highly repetitive plant eukaryotic genome was obtained by using a hybrid method of assembly utilizing the ends of clones of a BAC library combined with paired and unpaired 454 Titanium pyrosequencing fragments summing up to 12× coverage.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec3.p2" name="article1.body1.sec3.p2" class="link-target"></a><p>The assembled portion (53%) of the cucumber nuclear genome sequence (without gaps), has more than 14× coverage with 454 Titanium reads. As it became clear from the ultralength (1–2 bp) INDELs polymorphism analysis made in this work, and according to the other researchers <a href="#pone.0022728-Wicker1" class="ref-tip">[50]</a>, <a href="#pone.0022728-Wheat1" class="ref-tip">[61]</a>, such a coverage rate, is sufficient to eliminate pyrosequencing specific errors. Because the presented result of cucumber B10 line genome assembly is practically identical to those of American Gy14 line (36× coverage) <a href="#pone.0022728-Cavagnaro1" class="ref-tip">[54]</a> <a href="http://www.phytozome.net/cucumber.php">http://www.phytozome.net/cucumber.php</a>) and Chinese 9930 line (70× coverage) <a href="#pone.0022728-Huang1" class="ref-tip">[13]</a>, it seems that chosen whole genome sequencing method was the most effective one to get the first quality draft sequence of cucumber genome. By cross mapping each other assembly versions (Celera vs. Celera/Arachne), it was possible to anchor more Celera/Arachne contigs (the most genome representative) to the chromosomes. The assembled genome served also for de-novo gene prediction, functional annotations of genes and comparative genomics studies. The average contig size of 12 Kbp and especially almost 95% of genome contained in contigs longer than average gene length of 4 Kbp, gives the possibility to discover almost all protein coding genes.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec3.p3" name="article1.body1.sec3.p3" class="link-target"></a><p>The Darwinian fitness of individual species and/or lines could be determined by a species-specific set of genes and also by the whole regulatory network formed by the specific activation/deactivation of TRFs, which in turn interact with specific CREs residing within gene promoters.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec3.p4" name="article1.body1.sec3.p4" class="link-target"></a><p>We observed that the European cultivar in comparison to Chinese Long cultivar (<a href="#pone-0022728-t003">Table 3</a> and <a href="#pone.0022728.s030">Table S13a</a>) has increased number of genes involved in photosynthesis, respiration, sugar metabolism, chlorophyll degradation and regulation of gene expression. This might indicate for adaptation to the temperate European climate conditions (lower average light intensity and grow temperatures). On the other hand Chinese Long cultivar has more genes related to photooxidative stress tolerance (UV and high light irradiation), higher non-optimal temperatures tolerance (e.g. heat shock proteins) and pathogen attack resistance (e.g. peroxidases and glycoproteins), therefore is better adapted to the subtropical climate conditions. We have also found that in line B10 of the European cucumber cultivar, a much larger number of genes take part in ammonium ion assimilation. This is expected to compensate for the limited ability to assimilate nitrate ions <a href="#pone.0022728-Bloom1" class="ref-tip">[84]</a>, <a href="#pone.0022728-Bloom2" class="ref-tip">[85]</a>, probably as a result of higher levels of continuous CO<sub>2</sub> emission in Europe till the 90's of the 20<sup>th</sup> century. Industrial era in Europe started at the end of 18th century, but taking into consideration that significantly higher carbon dioxide atmospheric emission started together with wide availability of internal combustion engine at the end of 19th, it could be concluded that mentioned adaptive genomic changes arose in about 100 generations. Unicellular yeast, needs only about 2–3-times less generations to acquire new genomic adaptations <a href="#pone.0022728-Colson1" class="ref-tip">[19]</a>. It seems like the time needed by multicellular cucumber plants to adapt to new environmental conditions is extremely short and constitute the new evidence of “evolution in action”. Recently the similarly fast environmental adaptation was reported for Atlantic tomcod (<em>Microgadus tomcod</em>) living in Hudson River, NY, USA <a href="#pone.0022728-Wirgin1" class="ref-tip">[86]</a>.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec3.p5" name="article1.body1.sec3.p5" class="link-target"></a><p>Rearrangements of inversions and translocations type which differ two cucumber genotypes, growing in extremely diverse environmental conditions, could have arose as a result of evolutionary adaptation to specific conditions. Functional comparative analysis between cucumber genotypes, showed that 9930 line genome contains 4-times more copies of peroxidase genes and 3-times more copies of two dehydrogenase genes, which are crucial for oxidative stress resistance in plants <a href="#pone.0022728-Yamasaki1" class="ref-tip">[17]</a>. Like it was reported in pathogen <em>Cryptococcus neoformansand</em> and in yeast, oxidative abiotic stress caused by high temperature, UV light or hydrogen peroxide result in wide range of chromosomal rearrangements (translocations, deletions, inversions, amplifications, chromosome fusions or aneuploidy), which are stabilized over generations and lead to resistance's acquisition <a href="#pone.0022728-Ragu1" class="ref-tip">[18]</a>, <a href="#pone.0022728-Sun1" class="ref-tip">[87]</a>. It was shown in cucumber, that UV-B radiation leads to higher ploidy and bigger size of cells surrounding leaves' trichomes. Additionally the higher expression of peroxidase and dehydrogenase genes required for UV radiation's protection, was noted <a href="#pone.0022728-Yamasaki1" class="ref-tip">[17]</a>. In turn, the B10 line increased number of genes responsible for effective energy management, under relatively low temperature and low sunlight intensity conditions, may be the result of chromosomal rearrangements and amplifications of specific genome regions, caused by the necessity to adapt to a given environment. This has been demonstrated in yeast maintained for many generations under conditions of lower availability of nutrients, in particular glucose <a href="#pone.0022728-Coyle1" class="ref-tip">[14]</a>, <a href="#pone.0022728-Colson1" class="ref-tip">[19]</a>, <a href="#pone.0022728-Dunham1" class="ref-tip">[88]</a>), and in flax in response to low temperatures <a href="#pone.0022728-Cullis1" class="ref-tip">[20]</a>.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec3.p6" name="article1.body1.sec3.p6" class="link-target"></a><p>The structural differences leading to changes in gene copy numbers were showed recently in corn between 33 genotypes <a href="#pone.0022728-SwansonWagner1" class="ref-tip">[89]</a>. The rearrangements (CNVs – copy number variations and PAVs - presence absence variations) were related to the acclimations of the genotypes to different environmental conditions. Recently, large (1 Mbp) chromosomal rearrangements (mostly inversions and translocations) between 17 human genomes sequences were reported. Detection of those extensive rearrangements was enabled by sequencing of 14 million fosmid (40 Kbp insert) library ends <a href="#pone.0022728-Kidd1" class="ref-tip">[90]</a>. The other work, reported occurrence of large chromosomal duplications in genomes of breast cancer cells. That finding was achievable because of the use of new methodology named array painting <a href="#pone.0022728-Howarth1" class="ref-tip">[91]</a>. Like it was noted by Swanson-Wagner et al., 2010 <a href="#pone.0022728-SwansonWagner1" class="ref-tip">[89]</a>, till now it was believed, that the same species' organisms should have similar genomes. On the other hand, authors of the recent findings of extensive structural genome differences between whole human genomes or in breast cancer cells, remark that discovery of such genomic changes was not earlier possible, because the broad used methods like FISH (fluorescent in-situ hybridization), CGH (comparative genomic hybridization), SNP microarrays or genome resequencing, do not give such a possibility <a href="#pone.0022728-Kidd1" class="ref-tip">[90]</a>, <a href="#pone.0022728-Howarth1" class="ref-tip">[91]</a>. It is important to note that in the other cucumber genome project (Gy14 line) the rearrangements were not reported till now. As it is written at the web address <a href="http://www.phytozome.net/cucumber.php">http://www.phytozome.net/cucumber.php</a>, the “comparison with the genetic map of cucumber supports the long-range accuracy of the assembly”. We believe that the main reason why they had not found any rearrangements was the use of genetic map containing the markers and their locations common for both Chinese and American background as it cames out from paper of Ren et al. <a href="#pone.0022728-Ren1" class="ref-tip">[69]</a>. Such a methodology assumed that the rearrangements are not possible between different genotypes of the same species. This assumption is not only conflicting with our results but also with the very recent paper by Zhao et al. <a href="#pone.0022728-Zhao1" class="ref-tip">[66]</a> where a large rearrangements of satellite and rRNA genes were reported between <em>Cucumis sativus</em> var. sativus and <em>Cucumis sativus</em> var. hardwickii.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec3.p7" name="article1.body1.sec3.p7" class="link-target"></a><p>Regarding to the above comment, it seems that the discovery of global genomic changes (rearrangements) between two cucumber genomes, were possible only because of availability of two genomes sequences generated de-novo together with the use of diverse genome background genetic map.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec3.p8" name="article1.body1.sec3.p8" class="link-target"></a><p>The differences, we identified by comparing the genomes of two cucumber cultivars at the levels of chromosomes and genes, suggest that specific environmental and evolutionary adaptations of line B10 and line 9930 have occurred in response to the temperate Northern Europe and subtropical southeast Asia climate conditions, respectively, which are different then the Himalayan climate of its origin <a href="#pone.0022728-Sebastian1" class="ref-tip">[92]</a>.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec3.p9" name="article1.body1.sec3.p9" class="link-target"></a><p>Reported in this work, extensive and numerous interchromosomal rearrangements between two cultivars of the same crop species is the novel evidence. It should also be noted, that beside such rearrangements, all of the genotypes are fertile. Though the observed genome rearrangements need further and deeper analysis, it could be concluded that those whole chromosomes shifts could represent adaptive changes, because are accepted and heritable. It could be also supposed that the view of evolutionary genomic adaptations emerging from the previous comparative genomics studies based on i.e. resequencing or hybridization data, is at least only partial and could lead to erroneous conclusions about the real mechanisms of organisms adaptive changes.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec3.p10" name="article1.body1.sec3.p10" class="link-target"></a><p>Differences in expression of orthologous genes in the two closely related species <em>D. melanogaster</em> and <em>D. simulans</em> can be explained in most cases by the loss or gain of CREs in promoter sequences <a href="#pone.0022728-Wittkopp1" class="ref-tip">[93]</a>. Furthermore, Kasowski with colleagues (2010) <a href="#pone.0022728-Kasowski1" class="ref-tip">[94]</a> showed that binding sides of RNA polymerase II and nuclear factor κB differed significantly between human individuals. Binding differences were linked to SNPs and were often correlated with altered gene expression in humans <a href="#pone.0022728-Kasowski1" class="ref-tip">[94]</a>. Zhang with colleagues (2004) <a href="#pone.0022728-Zhang1" class="ref-tip">[95]</a> reported that freezing-sensitive tomato plants posses a functional CBF/DREB response pathway, but CBF regulon differs considerably from that of freezing-tolerant <em>A. thaliana</em>. Observed differences can be explained by species-specific distribution of functional DRE/CRT elements within promoters of orthologous genes, or by mutations in proteins required for CBF/DREB activity. Moreover, transcriptional variation of auxin responses in seven natural accessions of <em>A. thaliana</em> was observed recently by Delkler with colleagues (2010) <a href="#pone.0022728-Delker1" class="ref-tip">[96]</a>.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec3.p11" name="article1.body1.sec3.p11" class="link-target"></a><p>It was shown that CRE turnover and shuffling <a href="#pone.0022728-Weirauch1" class="ref-tip">[97]</a>, and transcriptional rewiring <a href="#pone.0022728-Schmidt1" class="ref-tip">[98]</a> often occurs in genomes of animals and yeasts over evolutionary time. However, in some cases these alterations do not affect gene expression <a href="#pone.0022728-Weirauch1" class="ref-tip">[97]</a>. In our work we also observed substantial differences in CRE content between all analyzed species and varieties. For example we found significant change in ABRE and ERE content between the genes of <em>O. sativa</em> and the other species. This may be partly explained by the higher GC content in <em>O.</em> sativa <a href="#pone.0022728-Yu2" class="ref-tip">[99]</a> and/or the role of ethylene in adaptation to root hypoxia conditions <a href="#pone.0022728-Muhlenbock1" class="ref-tip">[21]</a>. We also identified significant differences in CRE distributions (<a href="#pone-0022728-g002">Fig. 2A</a>, <a href="#pone.0022728.s013">Fig. S10</a> and <a href="#pone.0022728.s001">Dataset S1</a>) between the two lines of <em>C. sativus</em> (B10 and 9930). Moreover, we also examined the genes with ABRE, DRE and ERE in their promoters to determine if they belong to the same functional groupings in the analyzed species, we found that only a small fraction of the groups of orthologous genes with the highest sequence similarity in <em>A. thaliana</em>, <em>O. sativa</em>, <em>C. sativus</em> (B10 and 9930) and <em>P. trichocarpa</em> have the same CRE profiles in their promoters (<a href="#pone-0022728-g002">Fig. 2A</a>, <a href="#pone.0022728.s001">Dataset S1</a>). These results suggest that, each species and/or varieties has a specific profile of CRE content in gene promoters which actually determines specific changes in gene expression profiles (regulons) selected in response to the similar changes in environmental conditions (e.g. not optimal temperatures or water uptake limitations). We showed that non-acclimated <em>Arabidopsis</em> seedlings displayed better freezing tolerance after ABA treatment, while cucumber did not. This could be due to species-specific set of genes controlled by both ABRE and DRE and ABA specific response in the above species (<a href="#pone-0022728-g002">Fig. 2A</a>). Our ABA-treatment experiments (<a href="#pone-0022728-g002">Fig. 2B</a>) together with <em>in silico</em> analysis of CRE shuffling (<a href="#pone-0022728-g002">Fig. 2A</a>) explains why <em>C. sativus</em> is much more susceptible for cold and chilling stresses than <em>A. thaliana</em>. The large changes in sequences of promoters of orthologous genes in <em>C. sativus</em> are due to approximately a 3-fold greater number of SNPs and INDELs in promoters relative to exons and introns of the same gene and genotype (<a href="#pone-0022728-t005">Table 5</a>). In <em>P. trichocarpa</em> the number of polymorphisms was also found to be about 2-fold higher in non-coding sequences than in exons <a href="#pone.0022728-Tuskan1" class="ref-tip">[100]</a>. This indicate flexibility of gene' promoter sequences <a href="#pone.0022728-Tirosh1" class="ref-tip">[101]</a>, <a href="#pone.0022728-Tuch1" class="ref-tip">[102]</a>. Though our results still need to be confirmed in future experiments, these could indicate that rapid adaptation of a line or variety to new environmental conditions may take place at the level of the regulation of gene expression by changing interactions in a transcriptional network without loss of functionality of particular genes. It is possible that due to the large variability in promoter sequences (CRE shuffling) new lines/varieties may be formed to adapt to new ecological niches.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec3.p12" name="article1.body1.sec3.p12" class="link-target"></a><p>In conclusion, adaptation of plants to new environmental conditions may occur on the level of changes in gene dosage and gene quality, but the rapid adjustment probably occurs by rewiring of gene regulatory networks driven by CRE shuffling. We suggest that through evolution, eukaryotic organisms have been equipped with a high degree of freedom with respect to the variability of promoters, in terms of regulatory elements, and chromosomal rearrangements that allow for formation of new lines/varieties and species adapted to new ecological niches.</p>
</div>

<div id="section4" class="section toc-section"><a id="s4" name="s4" data-toc="s4" class="link-target" title="Materials and Methods"></a><h2>Materials and Methods</h2>
<div id="section1" class="section toc-section"><a id="s4a" name="s4a" class="link-target" title="Plant material"></a>
<h3>Plant material</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec4.sec1.p1" name="article1.body1.sec4.sec1.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>The plant material was the highly inbred (20 generations) monoecious B10 line of the Borszczagowski cucumber, an old field cultivar. The plants were cultivated in a greenhouse during the summer. Material for analysis was obtained from plants cultivated in a greenhouse under controlled photoperiod conditions 16 h/8 h day/night, at a temperature of 25°C–27°C during the day and 18°C–20°C at night. The intensity of the light was 1500 µmol (quantum) m<sup>−2</sup>·s<sup>−1</sup>. Young leaves with a surface area of 2–3 cm<sup>2</sup> were collected early in the morning directly after 24 hours of darkness, frozen in liquid nitrogen and stored at −74°C. DNA for genomic sequencing was isolated using the GenElute Plant Genomic DNA Purification Kit as recommended by the producer (Sigma Aldrich, St. Louis, MO, USA).</p>
</div>

<div id="section2" class="section toc-section"><a id="s4b" name="s4b" class="link-target" title="BAC library"></a>
<h3>BAC library</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec4.sec2.p1" name="article1.body1.sec4.sec2.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>The clones of long cucumber genome fragments used in this work were derived from two BAC libraries, both prepared in the pCC1BAC vector. Among 33,792 available clones 3,072 were amplified in <em>E. coli</em> EPI300 cells <a href="#pone.0022728-Gutman1" class="ref-tip">[103]</a> and the remaining clones were amplified in <em>E. coli</em> DH10B cells (Amplicon Express, Pullman, WA, USA). Genomic DNA of clones in EPI300 was previously treated with the <em>Hind</em>III restriction endonuclease. The remaining inserts were derived from cleavage with <em>Mbo</em>I and were ligated into the vector cleaved with <em>BamH</em>I.</p>
</div>

<div id="section3" class="section toc-section"><a id="s4c" name="s4c" class="link-target" title="BES sequences"></a>
<h3>BES sequences</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec4.sec3.p1" name="article1.body1.sec4.sec3.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>A total of 33,792 BAC clones were used for analysis giving in effect 67,584 BES sequences which were sequenced by the Sanger method using an ABI 3730xl capillary sequencer (Beckman Coulter Genomics, formerly Agencourt Bioscience Corporation, Danvers, MA, USA) and standard M13 Forward and M13 Reverse primers.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec4.sec3.p2" name="article1.body1.sec4.sec3.p2" class="link-target"></a><p>Purification of 67,584 BESs to filter out the low quality sequences and the vector and bacterial sequences was performed using Lucy v1.20 <a href="#pone.0022728-Chou1" class="ref-tip">[104]</a> and BLAST <a href="#pone.0022728-Altschul1" class="ref-tip">[105]</a> software. To remove non-nuclear sequences from further analysis, the BES sequences were aligned (using BLAST) to the cucumber chloroplast genome sequences (AJ970307 <a href="#pone.0022728-Plader1" class="ref-tip">[106]</a>) and to the set of plant mitochondrial sequences available in GenBank.</p>
</div>

<div id="section4" class="section toc-section"><a id="s4d" name="s4d" class="link-target" title="Genome sequencing"></a>
<h3>Genome sequencing</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec4.sec4.p1" name="article1.body1.sec4.sec4.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>Sequencing of BAC library end sequences (BES) were done by the Sanger method using an ABI 3730xl capillary sequencer (Beckman Coulter Genomics, Danvers, MA, USA) and standard M13 Forward and M13 Reverse primers. The whole genome shotgun sequencing was performed by the pyrosequencing method using XLR Titanium reagents (454 Life Sciences, a Roche company, Branford, CT, USA). Sequencing of random fragments described as UNpaired (UN) given 8× genome coverage and of fragments described as PAired ends (PA) produced 4× genome coverage with an average distance of a pair of genomic sequences of 3 Kbp. Paired fragments were connected to each other by a so-called FLX type linker (44 bases) with the sequence <span class="gene">5′-GTTGGAACCGAAAGGGTTTGAATTCAAACCCTTTCGGTTCCAAC-3′</span>. Preparation of the fragment libraries was performed according to the procedures of 454 Life Sciences. Titanium read lengths were characterized after processing by the sffinfo script from the Newbler ver. 2.0.00.20 package (454 Life Sciences) and, for paired libraries, rejecting the linker sequences using the script sff_extract 0.2.3 (<a href="http://bioinf.comav.upv.es/sff_extract/index.html">http://bioinf.comav.upv.es/sff_extract/index.html</a>).</p>
</div>

<div id="section5" class="section toc-section"><a id="s4e" name="s4e" class="link-target" title="Additional cucumber sequences"></a>
<h3>Additional cucumber sequences</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec4.sec5.p1" name="article1.body1.sec4.sec5.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>In addition to all of the above mentioned sequences, we used 1,781 paired and unpaired Sanger generated (Beckman Coulter Genomics, Danvers, MA, USA) (accession numbers: ET203886–ET203889, FH937590–FH937636, FI136294–FI136299, FI160638–FI160866, ES882265–ES883262) sequences which found to be polymorphic among near isogenic lines of cucumber differing in sex phenotype <a href="#pone.0022728-Witkowicz1" class="ref-tip">[107]</a>–<a href="#pone.0022728-Przybecki2" class="ref-tip">[109]</a>.</p>
</div>

<div id="section6" class="section toc-section"><a id="s4f" name="s4f" class="link-target" title="Genome assembly"></a>
<h3>Genome assembly</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec4.sec6.p1" name="article1.body1.sec4.sec6.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>The genome sequence was obtained after hybrid assembly by a combination of the Celera Assembler ver. 5.4 <a href="#pone.0022728-Miller1" class="ref-tip">[52]</a> and Arachne ver. 3.0 <a href="#pone.0022728-Jaffe1" class="ref-tip">[110]</a> programs. The version A assembly was done with Titanium and Sanger reads using the Celera program. The version B hybrid assembly was done by Arachne program using the sequence contigs obtained from assembly of solely pyrosequenced reads by the Celera program (version B step 1) together with BES sequences. Prior to the Arachne assembly (version B step 2), the version B step 1 sequence contigs were cut into fragments of an average length of 800 bp overlapping by 100 bp.. Moreover, information about the Celera scaffolds was maintained in the form of “artificial” paired ends (forward and reverse) with an average length of 800 bp.</p>
</div>

<div id="section7" class="section toc-section"><a id="s4g" name="s4g" class="link-target" title="Characterization of the genome on the basis of BESs and genomic sequences"></a>
<h3>Characterization of the genome on the basis of BESs and genomic sequences</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec4.sec7.p1" name="article1.body1.sec4.sec7.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>To identify and classify microsatellite sequences, MISA software (http:// <a href="http://pgrc.ipk-gatersleben.de/misa/misa.html">http://pgrc.ipk-gatersleben.de/misa/misa.html</a>) was used taking into consideration sequences of mono- to octanucleotides with a minimal length of 12 nt and the minimal number of 3 repeats units. Repetitive plant elements were identified and classified on the basis of aligning BES sequences to the Michigan State University database of Repetitive Plant Sequences <a href="#pone.0022728-Ouyang1" class="ref-tip">[111]</a>. Similarities were analyzed by the tBlastx algorithm with the threshold value 1e-05. The database of repetitive sequences in the cucumber genome was created <em>de novo</em> using the RepeatModeler program (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler.html">http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler.html</a>) on the basis of nuclear BES sequences and the repeat database Repbase Update <a href="#pone.0022728-Jurka1" class="ref-tip">[112]</a>. Masking of repetitive sequences was performed using the RepeatMasker program (<a href="http://repeatmasker.org">http://repeatmasker.org</a>). The above-mentioned method of analyzing microsatellites and repetitive plant elements was also used for genomic contigs.</p>
</div>

<div id="section8" class="section toc-section"><a id="s4h" name="s4h" class="link-target" title="Mapping genome on chromosomes"></a>
<h3>Mapping genome on chromosomes</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec4.sec8.p1" name="article1.body1.sec4.sec8.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>In order to map the line B10 genomic sequences on seven cucumber chromosomes 1,883 available sequences of markers together with the information about their positions on the genetic map (<a href="http://cucumber.genomics.org.cn">http://cucumber.genomics.org.cn</a>), <a href="#pone.0022728-Ren1" class="ref-tip">[69]</a> were used. The mapping was performed using the BLAST (blastn, 1×10<sup>−20</sup>, &gt;30% marker coverage, &gt;90% similarity) and specific Arachne modules on sequences obtained after assembly versions A and B. The cross-aligment of sequences from version A and B, done with the MUMmer 3.22 <a href="#pone.0022728-Kurtz1" class="ref-tip">[113]</a> program, facilitated proper assigment (correct position and orientation) of both assembly version contigs to chromosomes.</p>
</div>

<div id="section9" class="section toc-section"><a id="s4i" name="s4i" class="link-target" title="Gene prediction"></a>
<h3>Gene prediction</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec4.sec9.p1" name="article1.body1.sec4.sec9.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>The small number of available full transcripts (mRNA) which, as of February 2010, was only about 450 or even ESTs (approximately 6500), does not enable us to perform a supervised training for conventional <em>ab initio</em> gene finders <a href="#pone.0022728-Haas1" class="ref-tip">[71]</a>. Therefore, in this work, we used a novel unsupervised training approach implemented in the GeneMark-ES software. In this method, the un-annotated assembled genome is used as the program input to generate in iterations the algorithm final parameters that are used to obtain gene predictions <a href="#pone.0022728-Lomsadze1" class="ref-tip">[70]</a>, <a href="#pone.0022728-TerHovhannisyan1" class="ref-tip">[73]</a>. The cucumber coding sequences available in databases were used only to check the correctness of the proposed gene structures.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec4.sec9.p2" name="article1.body1.sec4.sec9.p2" class="link-target"></a><p>Parameters of the <em>ab inito</em> gene finder were determined by unsupervised training on the assembled <em>C. sativus</em> contigs by the GeneMark-ES <a href="#pone.0022728-Lomsadze1" class="ref-tip">[70]</a>, <a href="#pone.0022728-TerHovhannisyan1" class="ref-tip">[73]</a> program. We used 3,008 contig sequences longer than 20,000 bp with a joint length of 109 Mbp; repetitive and TE sequences in these contigs were masked. <em>Ab initio</em> predictions made by GeneMark-ES were verified using 63,035 cucumber Unigene EST sequences (<a href="http://www.icugi.org/">http://www.icugi.org/</a>) and 422 cDNAs (<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov">http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov</a>), which were purified from vector contaminations by the SeqClean program (<a href="http://compbio.dfci.harvard.edu/tgi/software/">http://compbio.dfci.harvard.edu/tgi/software/</a>). To create a test set we aligned Unigene and cDNA sequences to the genome by the BLAT program <a href="#pone.0022728-Kent1" class="ref-tip">[114]</a>. We thus identified the sequences of transcripts containing complete genes with introns. After excising introns, the above transcript sequences were used to generate a model for predicting intronless genes using the GeneMark.hmm-P program (<a href="http://opal.biology.gatech.edu/GeneMark/">http://opal.biology.gatech.edu/GeneMark/</a>). This model was then used in GeneMark.hmm-P to identify the start and stop codons of the genes and to evaluate the gene structures, thereby completing the generation of the test set.</p>
<a id="article1.body1.sec4.sec9.p3" name="article1.body1.sec4.sec9.p3" class="link-target"></a><p>The species specific model used to predict cucumber genes was assessed based on the sensitivity and specificity of gene detection; it was modified (on the basis of alignment analyses performed by the BLAT program) and used for <em>de novo</em> prediction of gene structures on all contigs longer than 1000 bp masked for repetitive and TE sequences. To adjust for the specifics of the cucumber genome we modified the default parameters of the model with respect to the minimum and maximum intron lengths and maximum length of intergenic regions. These values were changed, respectively, from 20 nt to 60 nt and from 10,000 to 40,000 nt, for the two last variables.</p>
</div>

<div id="section10" class="section toc-section"><a id="s4j" name="s4j" class="link-target" title="Functional annotation"></a>
<h3>Functional annotation</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec4.sec10.p1" name="article1.body1.sec4.sec10.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>Polypeptides proposed as the result of structural annotation were aligned to the GenBank protein database with the blastp algorithm <a href="#pone.0022728-Altschul1" class="ref-tip">[105]</a> and the e-value threshold of 1×10<sup>−10</sup> using the BRAGoMap <a href="#pone.0022728-Stajich1" class="ref-tip">[115]</a>, <a href="#pone.0022728-Woycicki1" class="ref-tip">[116]</a> program. From the ten obtained results, similarities to undescribed or unknown proteins, i.e. putative proteins, unknown proteins, expressed proteins, and hypothetical proteins, were removed. Among the remaining results one protein each with the greatest similarity was left. The identifiers of these proteins were mapped to Uniprot identifiers from the iProClass <a href="#pone.0022728-Wu2" class="ref-tip">[117]</a> database and subsequently using the GORetriver <a href="#pone.0022728-McCarthy1" class="ref-tip">[118]</a> program onto the Gene Ontology Consortium <a href="#pone.0022728-Ashburner1" class="ref-tip">[74]</a> classification. A more general GOSlim classification was obtained after using the GOSlimViewer <a href="#pone.0022728-McCarthy1" class="ref-tip">[118]</a> program. The conversion to the GOSlim TAIR classification (<a href="http://www.arabidopsis.org">http://www.arabidopsis.org</a>) was also performed.</p>
</div>

<div id="section11" class="section toc-section"><a id="s4k" name="s4k" class="link-target" title="Comparative analysis of genome sequences of line B10 and 9930"></a>
<h3>Comparative analysis of genome sequences of line B10 and 9930</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec4.sec11.p1" name="article1.body1.sec4.sec11.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>Alignment of the genome sequence contigs of two genetically distant cucumber varieties as well as the analysis of SNP differences between them were performed using the MUMmer 3.22 program for contigs of a minimal length of 1,000 bp. Comparison of the placement of scaffolds of both cucumber lines on chromosomes and visualization of rearrangements were performed using the Mauve ver. 2.3.1 program <a href="#pone.0022728-Darling1" class="ref-tip">[119]</a>.</p>
</div>

<div id="section12" class="section toc-section"><a id="s4l" name="s4l" class="link-target" title="Identification of genes containing ABRE, DRE and ERE elements in their promoters"></a>
<h3>Identification of genes containing ABRE, DRE and ERE elements in their promoters</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec4.sec12.p1" name="article1.body1.sec4.sec12.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>In order to identify genes which contained in their promoter sequences elements of the ABRE (<span class="gene">ACGTGTC</span>), DRE (<span class="gene">RCCGAC</span>, where R = A,G) and ERE (<span class="gene">AGCCGCC</span>) types, fragments 1,000 bp upstream the start codon (ATG) were taken from databases: <a href="http://www.popgenie.org/popgenie1/">http://www.popgenie.org/popgenie1/</a> - <em>P. trichocarpa</em>, <a href="ftp://ftp.plantbiology.msu.edu/pub/data/Eukaryotic_Projects/o_sativa/annotation_dbs/">ftp://ftp.plantbiology.msu.edu/pub/data/Eukaryotic_Projects/o_sativa/annotation_dbs/</a> - <em>O. sativa</em>. Promoter sequences of the B10 and 9930 lines were isolated from genome sequences on the basis of information on the location of genes assigned to contigs after structural annotation. The <em>cis</em> regulatory ABRE, DRE and ERE elements and their combinations were localized in gene promoters of the above species using Perl scripts. Regulatory elements and their combinations in <em>A. thaliana</em> gene promoters were sought by the Patmatch program (<a href="http://www.arabidopsis.org/cgi-bin/patmatch/nph-patmatch.pl">http://www.arabidopsis.org/cgi-bin/patmatch/nph-patmatch.pl</a>).</p>
</div>

<div id="section13" class="section toc-section"><a id="s4m" name="s4m" class="link-target" title="Comparison of protein sequences of A. thaliana, P. trichocarpa, O. sativa, C. sativus lines B10 and 9930"></a>
<h3>Comparison of protein sequences of <em>A. thaliana</em>, <em>P. trichocarpa</em>, <em>O. sativa</em>, <em>C. sativus</em> lines B10 and 9930</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec4.sec13.p1" name="article1.body1.sec4.sec13.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>Amino acid sequences of gene products of the analyzed species were downloaded from available databases: <a href="http://www.popgenie.org/popgenie1/">http://www.popgenie.org/popgenie1/</a> - <em>P. trichocarpa</em>; <a href="ftp://ftp.arabidopsis.org/home/tair/Sequences/blast_datasets/TAIR9_blastsets/">ftp://ftp.arabidopsis.org/home/tair/Sequences/blast_datasets/TAIR9_blastsets/</a> – <em>A. thaliana</em>, <a href="ftp://ftp.plantbiology.msu.edu/pub/data/Eukaryotic_Projects/o_sativa/annotation_dbs/">ftp://ftp.plantbiology.msu.edu/pub/data/Eukaryotic_Projects/o_sativa/annotation_dbs/</a> - <em>O. sativa</em>. The cucumber lines B10 and 9930 amino acid sequences of gene products were obtained after the gene prediction. In order to identify groups of orthologous genes, all amino acid sequences of gene products of analyzed species were aligned to each other, we used the BLAST program and the blastp <a href="#pone.0022728-Altschul1" class="ref-tip">[105]</a> algorithm. Hits with an <em>e-value</em> higher than 10<sup>−5</sup> were rejected. For the prediction of groups of orthologous genes we used OrthoMCL program <a href="#pone.0022728-Li1" class="ref-tip">[120]</a>. Altogether we identified 27,674 groups of orthologous proteins. For further analysis we selected groups which contained at least one gene of each analyzed species or lineages, and complete promoter sequences for all genes. Altogether we described 5,971 groups of orthologous proteins for the <em>A. thaliana</em>, <em>O. sativa</em>, <em>P. trichocarpa</em> and <em>C. sativus</em>: lines B10 and 9930 (<a href="#pone.0022728.s001">Dataset S1</a>). Subsequently, on the basis of analysis of promoter sequences of these genes we described common and different groups of orthologous genes in these species and lineages with respect to the occurrence of regulatory elements ABRE, DRE and ERE and combinations thereof.</p>
</div>

<div id="section14" class="section toc-section"><a id="s4n" name="s4n" class="link-target" title="Functional classification of genes containing ABRE, DRE and ERE elements in their promoters"></a>
<h3>Functional classification of genes containing ABRE, DRE and ERE elements in their promoters</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec4.sec14.p1" name="article1.body1.sec4.sec14.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>The division of <em>A. thaliana</em>, <em>P. trichocarpa</em>, and <em>O. sativa</em> genes into appropriate structural and functional groups was performed on the basis of information obtained from the ftp server: <a href="http://www.popgenie.rg/popgenie1/">http://www.popgenie.rg/popgenie1/</a> for analysis of <em>P. trichocarpa</em>; <a href="ftp://ftp.plantbiology.msu.edu/pub/data/Eukaryotic_Projects/o_sativa/annotation_dbs/">ftp://ftp.plantbiology.msu.edu/pub/data/Eukaryotic_Projects/o_sativa/annotation_dbs/</a> for analysis of <em>O. sativa</em>; and <a href="ftp://ftp.arabidopsis.org/home/tair/Ontologies/Gene_Ontology/">ftp://ftp.arabidopsis.org/home/tair/Ontologies/Gene_Ontology/</a> for <em>A. thaliana</em>. The assignment of functional groups for <em>C. sativus</em> line B10 proteins was performed by the functional annotation described in the online methods. This division was then unified by manual mapping of numerical identifiers of Generic GO Annotations to the GO Slim TAIR category (<a href="http://www.arabidopsis.org">www.arabidopsis.org</a>). Genes of the analyzed species were divided, according to function and cellular localization, into appropriate groups: GO Cellular Component, GO Molecular Function and GO Biological Process. In gene promoter sequences for each group we have described the occurrence of <em>cis</em> regulatory elements ABRE, DRE, ERE and their combinations ABRE-DRE, ABRE-ERE, DRE-ERE, and ABRE-DRE-ERE in <em>A. thaliana</em>, <em>C. sativus</em> line B10, <em>O. sativa</em> and <em>P. trichocarpa</em>.</p>
</div>

<div id="section15" class="section toc-section"><a id="s4o" name="s4o" class="link-target" title="Identification of putative transcription factors from C. sativus line B10 and 9930"></a>
<h3>Identification of putative transcription factors from <em>C. sativus</em> line B10 and 9930</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec4.sec15.p1" name="article1.body1.sec4.sec15.p1" class="link-target"></a><p>In order to identify putative transcription factors of <em>C. sativus</em> line B10 and line 9930, amino acid sequences of gene products were scanned using PFAM <a href="#pone.0022728-Finn1" class="ref-tip">[121]</a>. Hits with an <em>e-value</em> higher than 10<sup>−5</sup> were rejected. Hits with the highest similarity to known Arabidopsis AREBs (Abscisic Acid–Responsive Element Binding Protein), ERFs (Ethylene-responsive Element-binding Factor) and DREBs/CBFs (DRE binding proteins) were aligned by ClustalW and phylogenetic trees using Neighbour-Joining method were prepared using MEGA 4 program <a href="#pone.0022728-Kumar1" class="ref-tip">[122]</a>.</p>
</div>

<div id="section16" class="section toc-section"><a id="s4p" name="s4p" class="link-target" title="ABA treatment and electrolyte leakage"></a>
<h3>ABA treatment and electrolyte leakage</h3>
<a id="article1.body1.sec4.sec16.p1" name="article1.body1.sec4.sec16.p1" class="link-target"></a><p><em>Arabidopsis</em> and cucumber seedlings were subjected to 200 µM Abscisic acid (ABA) (Sigma Aldrich, St. Louis, MO, USA) for 3 days. The last ABA treatment was made 3 hours before freezing treatment. Electrolyte leakage experiments were performed as previously described <a href="#pone.0022728-JagloOttosen1" class="ref-tip">[123]</a> with the following modifications. Detached <em>Arabidopsis</em> and cucumber leaves leaf were placed in test tube and submerged for one hour in −2°C bath containing water and propylene glycol, after which ice crystals were added to initialize crystallization. After one hour of incubation in −2°C samples were cooled to −4°C and kept for additional hour. Samples (at least five replicates for each data point) were thawed overnight in 4°C in 1.5 ml of distilled water. Electrolyte leakage was measured after addition to each sample of 18.5 ml of distilled water. To obtain total electrolyte leakage samples were frozen for at least one hour in −80°C and tested as above.</p>
</div>
</div>

Characteristics of BESs obtained from two BAC libraries of B10 line of cucumber. BESs were trimmed and screened for contamination with vector and bacterial genomes using Lucy v1.20 and BLAST. BLAST was also used to find chloroplast and mitochondrion genome sequences.</p>

Processing of 454 XLR Titanium 4× paired library fragments, recovered paired reads and also unpaired reads. Fragments from this library which had no linker at all and fragments with partial or multiple linkers were treated as unpaired reads (the same as all of the reads from the 8× unpaired library). The only paired reads were recovered from fragments containing full linker sequences.</p>
